The Two Respects of Predicating 'One'
Author clarifies that 'one' is said of both accident and subject in different respects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two respects in which the term 'one' is predicated of an accident and its subject.

Core Idea
'One' is said of the attribute as itself, and of the subject as possessing it.

This passage clarifies a subtle point in predication: the same term 'one' can be applied to both an accident and its underlying substance, but not in the same way. Understanding this distinction is critical for precise logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two predications
Find where the author says 'one' is said of whiteness and of the subject.
2
Determine the meaning in each case
When said of whiteness, 'one' refers to the whiteness itself; when said of the subject, it refers to the subject's possession of whiteness.
3
Apply the analogy
The author compares this to substance being said of man and of itself. Explain how the analogy reinforces the distinction.
4
Generalize
Formulate a general rule: a term can be predicated of an accident and of its subject in different respects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال إن البياض واحد وكان الواحد مما يقال على البياض وعلى موضوعه، فإن الواحد حينئذ لا يمتنع أن يقال على الموضوع قول «على»، وليس من جملة البياض ؛ لأن الواحد الذي قيل على البياض هو البياض. فإذن البياض في موضوعه فذلك الواحد ليس مقولا عليه حتى يكون من حيث ذلك البياض واحدًا؛ بل هو من جهة ذو واحد. وإن كان في نفسه واحدا فهو واحد أخرى فالواحد يقال على الموضوع في نفسه ويوجد فيه من جهة بياضه؛ إذ ذاك الواحد الذي هو البياض ليس هو الواحد الذي هو الموضوع؛ بل فيه، وهذا كالجوهر يقال على الإنسان ويقال على نفسه، والجوهر الذي هو نفسه لا يقال عليه؛ بل موجود فيه. وإن كان كوجود الجزء لا كوجود العرض فبين أنه لا يمتنع حينئذ أن الصفة مقولة عليه من جهة، ومقولة فيه من جهة. وأما إذا كان الوصف المقول على العرض خاصا به فإنه يكون موجودًا في الموضوع لا غير. وأما إذا قلت النسبة يجعل الطرف الأكبر موجودًا في الأوسط، فالمشهور فيه أنه تارة يحمل حمل «في» كالبياض في الققنَّسِ، والققنَّس على فَقنَّس ما، والبياض في فَقنَّس ما؛ وتارة لا يحمل كالجنس في الحيوان والحيوان على الإنسان، والجنس لا يحمل على الإنسان ويجب أن ننظر فيما ذكرناه أن الجنس لا يحمل على الحيوان الذي هو بعينه مقول على الإنسان فلا يكون الأوسط واحدا بعينه، وإنما يجب أن نحفظ وحده الأوسط فإنه إذا كان واحدًا كان الموجود فيه إذا كان وجوده كليا كان هو موجودًا في الطرف الأصغر على خلاف أن لا يكون فيه كليًّا؛ بل يكون في بعضه، فإنه لا يجب ذلك أصلا.
For it is said that whiteness is one, and 'one' is something said of whiteness and of its subject. Then 'one' in that case is not prevented from being said of the subject as a 'said-of' predication, while it is not part of whiteness; because the 'one' that is said of whiteness is whiteness itself. Therefore, whiteness is in its subject, so that 'one' is not predicated of it such that it is one by virtue of that whiteness; rather, it is from a certain aspect a possessor of one. And if it is one in itself, it is another 'one'. So 'one' is said of the subject in itself and exists in it by virtue of its whiteness; for that 'one' which is whiteness is not the 'one' which is the subject; rather, it is in it. This is like substance which is said of man and said of itself, but the substance which is itself is not said of it; rather, it exists in it. And if it is like the existence of a part, not like the existence of an accident, then it is clear that it is not impossible for the attribute to be said of it from one aspect and to be in it from another aspect. As for when the description said of the accident is specific to it, then it exists in the subject only. As for when you say that the relation makes the major term exist in the middle term, the well-known view is that sometimes it predicates with an 'in' predication, like whiteness in the ? and the ? on a certain ?, and whiteness in a certain ?; and sometimes it does not predicate, like the genus in animal and animal on man, and the genus is not predicated on man. And we must consider in what we have mentioned that the genus is not predicated on animal which is itself said of man, so the middle term is not one and the same. Rather, we must preserve the unity of the middle term, for if it is one, then what exists in it, if its existence is universal, then it exists in the minor term, contrary to its not being universal in it; rather, it is in some of it, then that is not necessary at all.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification of how the term 'one' (الواحد) can be predicated of both an accident (like whiteness) and its subject. The author explains that 'one' can be said of whiteness itself and also of the subject that possesses whiteness, but in different respects. When 'one' is said of whiteness, it refers to the attribute itself; when said of the subject, it refers to the subject being one by virtue of that whiteness. This is analogous to substance being said of man and of itself. The author then distinguishes between two types of predication: 'said of' (مقول على) and 'existing in' (موجود فيه). An attribute like whiteness is 'in' its subject, but when a description is specific to the accident, it only exists in the subject. The text then discusses how the relation (النسبة) makes the major term exist in the middle term in syllogisms, noting that sometimes the predication is 'in' (like whiteness in a body) and sometimes it is 'genus-species' (like animal of man). A key point is the requirement for the middle term to be one and the same; if the genus is not predicated of the animal that is itself said of man, then the middle term is not truly one. The passage underscores the importance of precise logical terminology for correct reasoning.

Key Terms
الواحد
مقول على
موجود فيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when 'one' is said of whiteness and of its subject, which statement is correct?
A) 'One' is said of both in exactly the same way.
B) 'One' is said of whiteness as an attribute and of the subject as possessing that attribute.
C) 'One' is only said of the subject, not of whiteness.
D) 'One' is said of whiteness but not of the subject because the subject is multiple.

Key Takeaway
The term 'one' is predicated of an accident as the attribute itself, and of the subject as the possessor of that attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the accident 'whiteness' and a white subject. Write a short explanation of how 'one' is said of each, using the terminology maqul 'ala and mawjud fihi from the passage.
?
Find a similar example in Islamic theology: how the term 'living' is said of Allah and of a human. Explain whether it is said in the same respect or not.

The Unity of the Middle Term in Syllogisms
Author explains the condition that the middle term must be one for valid inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether the middle term in a categorical syllogism is one and the same, and recognize violations.

Core Idea
For a valid syllogism, the middle term must be a single term predicated identically in both premises.

After discussing predication of 'one', the author turns to a critical condition for syllogistic validity: the middle term must be one. This lesson unpacks why unity of the middle term is essential and how violations can lead to fallacious reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
Recall that the middle term appears in both premises and connects major and minor terms.
2
Understand the condition of unity
According to the passage, the middle term must be one and the same; if it is not (e.g., because of different predication modes), the syllogism fails.
3
Analyze the example
The author gives an example where the genus is not predicated of animal that is said of man, so the middle term is not truly one. Explain this.
4
Apply to standard syllogisms
Construct a valid first-figure syllogism and verify the middle term is one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال إن البياض واحد وكان الواحد مما يقال على البياض وعلى موضوعه، فإن الواحد حينئذ لا يمتنع أن يقال على الموضوع قول «على»، وليس من جملة البياض ؛ لأن الواحد الذي قيل على البياض هو البياض. فإذن البياض في موضوعه فذلك الواحد ليس مقولا عليه حتى يكون من حيث ذلك البياض واحدًا؛ بل هو من جهة ذو واحد. وإن كان في نفسه واحدا فهو واحد أخرى فالواحد يقال على الموضوع في نفسه ويوجد فيه من جهة بياضه؛ إذ ذاك الواحد الذي هو البياض ليس هو الواحد الذي هو الموضوع؛ بل فيه، وهذا كالجوهر يقال على الإنسان ويقال على نفسه، والجوهر الذي هو نفسه لا يقال عليه؛ بل موجود فيه. وإن كان كوجود الجزء لا كوجود العرض فبين أنه لا يمتنع حينئذ أن الصفة مقولة عليه من جهة، ومقولة فيه من جهة. وأما إذا كان الوصف المقول على العرض خاصا به فإنه يكون موجودًا في الموضوع لا غير. وأما إذا قلت النسبة يجعل الطرف الأكبر موجودًا في الأوسط، فالمشهور فيه أنه تارة يحمل حمل «في» كالبياض في الققنَّسِ، والققنَّس على فَقنَّس ما، والبياض في فَقنَّس ما؛ وتارة لا يحمل كالجنس في الحيوان والحيوان على الإنسان، والجنس لا يحمل على الإنسان ويجب أن ننظر فيما ذكرناه أن الجنس لا يحمل على الحيوان الذي هو بعينه مقول على الإنسان فلا يكون الأوسط واحدا بعينه، وإنما يجب أن نحفظ وحده الأوسط فإنه إذا كان واحدًا كان الموجود فيه إذا كان وجوده كليا كان هو موجودًا في الطرف الأصغر على خلاف أن لا يكون فيه كليًّا؛ بل يكون في بعضه، فإنه لا يجب ذلك أصلا.
For it is said that whiteness is one, and 'one' is something said of whiteness and of its subject. Then 'one' in that case is not prevented from being said of the subject as a 'said-of' predication, while it is not part of whiteness; because the 'one' that is said of whiteness is whiteness itself. Therefore, whiteness is in its subject, so that 'one' is not predicated of it such that it is one by virtue of that whiteness; rather, it is from a certain aspect a possessor of one. And if it is one in itself, it is another 'one'. So 'one' is said of the subject in itself and exists in it by virtue of its whiteness; for that 'one' which is whiteness is not the 'one' which is the subject; rather, it is in it. Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for the middle term in a valid syllogism?
A) It must be predicated universally.

Truth Conditions of Indefinite Propositions and Their Relations
Explains truth conditions for indefinite propositions and subaltern/contrary relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth conditions of indefinite propositions and identify subaltern and contrary pairs among them.

Core Idea
Indefinite propositions' truth depends on predicate distribution, not quantification.

Indefinite propositions lack explicit quantifiers, but their logical behavior is not arbitrary. In this passage, the author systematically lays out when each indefinite form is true or false, and shows how they relate to each other. Understanding these conditions is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology, where indefinite statements often carry implicit universal or particular force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logical form
Each indefinite proposition has a specific form: simple affirmative ('S is P'), simple negative ('S is not P'), privative affirmative ('S is not non-P'), privative negative ('S is non-P'), and their modified versions. The passage lists six forms. Determine which form you have.
Forms: 'S is just', 'S is not unjust', 'S is not non-just', 'S is not just', 'S is unjust', 'S is not-just'.
2
Determine truth conditions
For each form, the truth depends on the actual distribution of the predicate among the subject. For example, 'S is just' is true if at least one S is just; false if no S is just. The passage provides conditions for each form based on whether some, all, or none of the subject are just or unjust.
The text says these conditions are 'known by reflection' but gives explicit examples.
3
Recognize subaltern pairs
Subaltern pairs (المضالعات) are those where one proposition implies the other. The passage states that if a more specific proposition is true, the more general is also true. For example, if 'Some S are just' is true, then 'All S are just' is not necessarily true, but the indefinite forms relate similarly. Identify pairs where truth of one guarantees truth of the other.
The author notes that subalterns all agree in truth when some are just and some are not.
4
Identify contrary pairs
Contrary pairs (المقاطرات) are those that cannot both be true. The passage lists several, such as simple affirmative and privative affirmative, which may both be true under certain conditions but are generally contraries. Determine which pairs cannot be simultaneously true.
The passage gives conditions for agreement (both true) between certain forms, which helps identify when they fail to be contrary.
5
Apply to Islamic science examples
Use the truth conditions to analyze arguments in fiqh and kalam. For instance, the indefinite proposition 'The prayer is valid in cases' can be assessed for truth or falsehood based on the distribution of validity among prayers, and its relation to other forms can help derive rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زيد ليس يوجد جائرا يكذب إذا كان جائرًا فقط زيد يوجد جائرا يصدق إذا كان جائرًا فقط فقد تبين أن الاثنين حالهما عند العدميتين كحال الإيجاب والسلب عندهما، والاثنين ليس كذلك، ولا يستراب في أن ما هو أخص صدقًا من شيء فينقضه أعم صدقاً من بعض ذلك الشيء؛ وذلك لأن الأخص صدقا هو الأعم كذبًا، والأعم صدقاً هو الأخص كذبًا فينقض لازم الأخص من غیر انعكاس وهو ملزوم للنقض الأخص من غير انعكاس على ما عرف وأما لوح المهملات فهذا: الإنسان يوجد عادلاً في موالي الإنسان ليس يوجد جائرًا الإنسان ليس يوجد لا عادلاً الإنسان ليس يوجد عادلاً الإنسان ليس جائرًا الإنسان يوجد لا عادلاً وأما اعتبار الصدق والكذب في الكل فإنه من جملة ما يعرف بالتأمل فلا حاجة إلى التصريح بالذكر، وبالجملة فالمضالعات كلها تجتمع على الصدق إذا كان بعض عادل و بعض ليس. وأما المقاطرات فإن الموجبة البسيطة والعدمية تتفقان إذا كان بعض عادلاً وبعض جائرًا، والموجبة البسيطة والموجبة المعدولة إذا كان بعض عادلاً؛ والبواقي موجودون فقط ما كانوا. وأما السالبة البسيطة والسالبة العدمية فإنهما تتفقان إذا لم يكن فيهما عادل ولا جائر أصلا، أو كان بعض عادلاً وبعض جائرًا. وأما السالبة البسيطة والسالبة المعدولة فتتفقان إذا كانتا معدومتين أو بعض عادلاً وبعض غير عادل. وأما الموجبة العدمية والسالبة المعدولة فتتفقان إذا كان البعض جائرًا والبعض الآخر ما كان. وأما السالبة العدمية والموجبة المعدولة فتتفقان إذا لم يكن فيهما عادل ولا جائر، أو إذا كان بعض وبعض
Zayd is not unjust: it is false if he is only unjust. Zayd is unjust: it is true if he is only unjust. So it has become clear that the two [propositions] in relation to the privative [propositions] are like the affirmative and negative in relation to them, and the two are not like that. And there is no doubt that what is more specific in truth than a thing is refuted by what is more general in truth than some of that thing; because the more specific in truth is the more general in falsehood, and the more general in truth is the more specific in falsehood. So the necessary consequence of the more specific is refuted without conversion, and it is the necessary condition for the refutation of the more specific without conversion, as known. As for the table of indefinite propositions: Man is just in cases; Man is not unjust; Man is not not-just; Man is not just; Man is not unjust; Man is not-just. As for considering truth and falsehood in all, it is among things known by reflection, so there is no need to mention explicitly. In summary, all subalterns agree in truth if some are just and some are not. As for contraries: the simple affirmative and the privative agree if some are just and some are unjust; the simple affirmative and the modified affirmative agree if some are just; and the rest exist only as they were. As for the simple negative and the privative negative: they agree if there is neither just nor unjust at all, or if some are just and some are unjust. As for the simple negative and the modified negative: they agree if both are non-existent, or some are just and some are not just. As for the privative affirmative and the modified negative: they agree if some are unjust and the rest are not. As for the privative negative and the modified affirmative: they agree if there is neither just nor unjust, or if there is some and some.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage addresses the truth conditions and logical relations among indefinite (non-quantified) categorical propositions. It begins by illustrating with two simple propositions about Zayd: 'Zayd is not unjust' and 'Zayd is unjust'. The first is false if Zayd is only unjust (i.e., if he is unjust and nothing else), and the second is true under the same condition. This establishes a relation between the indefinite affirmative and negative. The author then clarifies that the two indefinite propositions (simple affirmative and simple negative) relate to 'privative' propositions (those with 'not' in the predicate term) in a manner analogous to how affirmative and negative relate to each other. The discussion moves to a general principle: a proposition that is more specific in truth (i.e., more restricted in its application) is refuted by a proposition that is more general in truth regarding some part of the subject. This is because the more specific in truth is more general in falsehood, and vice versa. This mirrors the logical subaltern relation between universal and particular. Next, a table of indefinite propositions is given: 'Man is just in cases', 'Man is not unjust', 'Man is not not-just', 'Man is not just', 'Man is not unjust', 'Man is not-just'. The table shows six distinct logical forms. The author then explains that the truth of these propositions depends on the actual distribution of 'just' and 'unjust' among individuals. 'Subalterns' (المضالعات) are pairs where one implies the other; they all agree in truth when some individuals are just and some are not. 'Contraries' (المقاطرات) are pairs that cannot both be true but can both be false. The author lists several pairs: simple affirmative and privative affirmative agree (i.e., are both true) when some are just and some unjust; simple affirmative and modified affirmative agree when some are just; simple negative and privative negative agree when none are just or unjust, or when some are just and some unjust; simple negative and modified negative agree when both are non-existent (i.e., the subject is empty) or some are just and some not; privative affirmative and modified negative agree when some are unjust and the rest are not; privative negative and modified affirmative agree when none are just or unjust, or when there is a mix. The passage thus systematically lays out the truth conditions for indefinite propositions, which is foundational for understanding the square of opposition for quantified propositions. The author notes that these truth conditions are known by reflection, requiring no explicit statement. In Islamic logic, such distinctions are vital for analyzing arguments in fiqh (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' vs. 'Some intoxicant is forbidden').

Key Terms
العدميتان
المضالعات
المقاطرات
الموجبة البسيطة
الموجبة المعدولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition do the simple negative and privative negative (indefinite) agree in truth?
A) When some individuals are just and some unjust.
B) When there is neither just nor unjust at all.
C) When some are just only.
D) When some are unjust only.

Key Takeaway
Indefinite propositions have distinct truth conditions based on predicate distribution, and understanding their subaltern and contrary relations is essential for logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an indefinite proposition about prayer (e.g., 'The prayer is valid in cases') and determine its truth condition based on the distribution of validity among prayers. Then identify its subaltern partner if the scenario involves some valid and some invalid prayers.
?
Given the indefinite propositions 'Man is just' and 'Man is not unjust', determine under what conditions they are both true, and explain whether they form a subaltern or contrary pair.

Understanding Contradiction and Contrariety in the Square of Opposition
Presents tables of contradictories for quantified propositions and explains truth conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify contradictory and contrary pairs of quantified categorical propositions and determine their truth conditions using the square of opposition.

Core Idea
Contraries can both be false; contradictories never share truth.

The square of opposition is a fundamental tool in Islamic logic for understanding how quantified propositions relate to each other. In this passage, the author presents the table of contradictories and explains when contrary affirmatives can both be false. Mastering these relations is key to performing valid immediate inferences and avoiding logical errors in theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four standard forms
From the passage, extract the four categorical propositions: 'Every man is just' (A), 'No man is just' (E), 'Some men are just' (I), 'Some men are not just' (O). Note the additional propositions with 'unjust' and 'not-just'.
The passage uses multiple predicates to illustrate relations.
2
Locate contradictory pairs
Find which pairs are contradictories: e.g., 'Every man is just' and 'Not every man is just' (A vs O). Verify that they always have opposite truth values.
Contradictories are the foundation of the square.
3
Analyze contrary affirmatives
Examine the statement: contrary affirmatives (e.g., 'Every man is just' and 'Every man is unjust') cannot both be true but can both be false. Identify when both are false: when some are just and some are unjust.
This is a key distinction from contradictories.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Translate the logical relations into a fiqh context: e.g., 'Every sale of wine is invalid' (A) and 'No sale of wine is invalid' (E) are contraries. Determine truth conditions if some sales are valid and some are not.
This shows practical use in Islamic law.

The Square of Contradictories for Modified Propositions
Author presents the square of contradiction for quantified propositions with modified predicates and explains truth conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the square of contradictories for quantified modified propositions and determine the truth conditions for each contradictory pair.

Core Idea
Contradictory affirmatives can both be false but never both true; contradictory negatives can both be true but never both false.

This passage from the logic text presents the square of contradictories for propositions whose predicates are modified (عدول). Understanding these relationships is crucial for evaluating arguments that use quantified statements with negative predicates. We will walk through the square and uncover the truth conditions for each pair, using the example of justice (عدل) and injustice.

Guided Path
1
Identify proposition types
The passage distinguishes four types: affirmative privative (موجبة عدمية), negative modified (سالبة معدولة), negative privative (سالبة عدمية), and affirmative modified (موجبة معدولة). Note the agreement conditions mentioned: the affirmative privative and negative modified agree when some are unjust and others are not; the negative privative and affirmative modified agree when there is no just or unjust, or a mix.
Privative predicates negate the quality (e.g., 'not-just'), while modified predicates use a negated term (e.g., 'unjust').
2
List the universal affirmative square
The passage gives six propositions for the universal case: 'Every man is just', 'Not every man is unjust', 'Not every man is just', 'Every man is unjust', 'Not every man is not-just', 'Every man is not-just'. Identify which are contradictory pairs: 'Every man is just' contradicts 'Not every man is just'; 'Every man is unjust' contradicts 'Not every man is unjust'; 'Every man is not-just' contradicts 'Not every man is not-just'.
Contradictories are opposites in truth value: one must be true and the other false in any given situation.
3
Test truth conditions for affirmatives
The author states that contradictory affirmatives (e.g., 'Every man is just' and 'Every man is not-just') never agree in truth but can both be false. When are they both false? When all men are nonexistent, or when some are just and some are not just. For example, if half the men are just and half are unjust, both universals are false because not every man is just and not every man is unjust.
This shows that universals can be false even if the subject exists; they require a homogeneous subject set.
4
Reverse the square for universal negatives
When the universal is negative (i.e., the quantifier is negative like 'not a single'), the square changes. The passage gives: 'Some men are just', 'Not a single man is just', 'Not a single man is unjust', 'Some men are unjust', 'Not a single man is not-just', 'Some men are not-just'. Here, contradictories are 'Not a single man is just' and 'Some men are just', etc. Negatives can both be true (e.g., when all men are nonexistent) but never both false.
The term 'not a single' (ولا واحد) is equivalent to 'no' in the sense of universal negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عادل. وأما الموجبة العدمية والسالبة المعدولة فتتفقان إذا كان البعض جائرًا والبعض الآخر ما كان. وأما السالبة العدمية والموجبة المعدولة فتتفقان إذا لم يكن فيهما عادل ولا جائر، أو إذا كان بعض وبعض، وأما لوح المحصورات المتناقضات فهكذا: كل إنسان يوجد عادلاً، ليس كل إنسان يوجد جائرًا، ليس كل إنسان يوجد عادلاً، كل إنسان يوجد جائرًا، ليس كل إنسان يوجد لا عادلاً، كل إنسان يوجد لا عادلاً. فاعتبر الصدق والكذب في الكل فإنك تعرفهما وتعرف أن الموجبات المقاطرة لا تجتمع البتة على الصدق لكن تجتمع على الكذب، إذا كان الكل معدومين أو إذا كان البعض عادلًا والبعض غير عادل ما كان، فإن قولنا كل إنسان يوجد عادلا يكذب، إذا كان بعضهم عادلًا فقط. وهناك يكذب كل إنسان يوجد جائرًا، وكذلك كل إنسان يوجد لا عادلا. وأما السوالب فإنها قد تجتمع على الصدق حيث تكذب الموجبات ولا تجتمعان على الكذب وإلا لاجتمع تناقضها على الخلاف: هذا إذا كانت الكلية موجبة. فأما إذا كانت سالبة فاللوح كذا: بعض الناس يوجد عادلاً، ليس ولا واحد من الناس عادلاً، ليس ولا واحد من الناس بجائر، بعض الناس يوجد جائرًا، ليس ولا واحد من الناس لا عادلاً، بعض الناس يوجد لا عادلاً.
'Adil. As for the affirmative privative and the negative modified, they agree when some are unjust and the others are what they were. Commentary
This passage defines the logical relations of contradictories for quantified propositions that have undergone modification ('adl) in the predicate. The author begins by stating agreement conditions between two types of modified propositions: the affirmative privative (موجبة عديمة) and the negative modified (سالبة معدولة) agree when some individuals are unjust and the others are something else; similarly, the negative privative and affirmative modified agree when there are neither just nor unjust individuals, or when there is a mix. Then the square of contradictories (لوح المتناقضات) is presented for the universal case, listing six propositions: the universal affirmative, its contradictory (not every), the universal negative, etc., including modified forms like 'not every man is not-just' and 'every man is not-just'. The author explains that contradictory affirmatives (الموجبات المقاطرة) never both be true, but can both be false, e.g., when all men are nonexistent or when some are just and some are not. For negatives, they can both be true when the affirmatives are false, but never both false, otherwise the contradictories would coincide. A second square is given for when the universal is negative, listing propositions with the quantifier 'some' and 'not a single'.

Key Terms
الموجبة العدمية (al-mūjaba al-ʿadamiyya): Affirmative proposition with a privative predicate (e.g., 'Every man is not-just') where the predicate negates a quality. [Note: No significant shift; this is standard terminology in logic for propositions with negative predicates.]
السالبة المعدولة (al-sālaba al-muʿaddala): Negative proposition with a modified predicate (e.g., 'No man is unjust') where the predicate is negated by modification rather than simple negation. [Note: No significant shift; consistent usage across classical logic texts.]
المتناقضات (al-mutanāqiḍāt): Contradictory propositions; pairs that cannot both be true and cannot both be false under the same conditions. [Note: Standard definition; stable across the tradition.]

Key Takeaway
Contradictory universal affirmatives can both be false when the subject set is mixed, while contradictory negatives can both be true when the subject is empty or mixed.

Truth Patterns in Universal Propositions with Modified Predicate
Author analyzes agreement of truth/falsity in contradictory and contrary pairs with modifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the truth conditions for contradictory, affirmative subcontrary, negative subcontrary, and contrary universal propositions with modified predicates.

Core Idea
Modifying the predicate reshapes the square of opposites—truth patterns differ by type.

In this passage, the author presents a square of opposites for universal propositions with a modified predicate. By examining six example propositions about justice, he shows how truth and falsehood relate across different pairs. This analysis reveals critical distinctions between contradiction, subcontrariety, and contrariety, especially when the predicate includes a modifier ('adl). Understanding these relations equips you to evaluate propositional truth in logical arguments within Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six propositions in the square of opposites
Look at the Arabic: three affirmative and three negative propositions, including those with modified predicates like 'not just'.
The six: كل إنسان يوجد عادلا, لا واحد من الإنسان يوجد عادلا, لا واحد من الإنسان يوجد جائرًا, كل إنسان يوجد جائرًا, لا واحد من الإنسان يوجد لا عادلا, كل إنسان يوجد لا عادلا.

Group them into contradictory pairs (e.g., first and second), affirmative subcontraries, negative subcontraries, and contraries as described by the author.
Contradictories: (1,2), (3,4), (5,6). Affirmative subcontraries: (1 and 5? Actually, author defines them separately.)
3
Determine truth agreement for each type
Apply the author's findings: contradictories never agree in truth but agree in falsehood; affirmative subcontraries never agree in truth but may agree in falsehood if subjects are non-existent or mixed; negative subcontraries agree in both truth and falsehood; contraries are treated as indeterminate.
The author specifically says 'مقاطرات إيجابية' never agree in truth but may agree in falsehood.
4
Explain the role of the modifier
The modified predicate ('adl) creates new proposition pairs beyond the standard four categoricals. Without it, the square would have only four propositions; with it, there are six, expanding logical relations.
The modifier changes 'just' to 'unjust' to 'not just', affecting truth conditions.

As for truth and falsehood in the universal [propositions], they are among what is known by reflection as well. Commentary
This passage classifies universal propositions based on their truth/falsehood behavior when the predicate is modified (عدول). These divisions arise from whether the predicate is modified ('adl). For example, 'Every human exists just' and 'No human exists just' are contradictories; they cannot both be true but can both be false. The lesson emphasizes that modifying the predicate creates these distinct logical relations.

Conditions for Equivalence of Modified and Unmodified Propositions
Author states conditions for equivalence with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the conditions under which a modified proposition is equivalent to an unmodified proposition in categorical logic.

Core Idea
Equivalent modified propositions require the subject to exist.

In logic, we often transform propositions by changing their quality or modifying the predicate. But such transformations are not always valid. The author specifies exactly when a proposition with a modified predicate (e.g., 'not just') is equivalent to one with a simple predicate (e.g., 'just'). The key condition is that the subject term must have existing referents. This lesson explains the conditions and provides a proof by contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions in the equivalence claim
Look at the quantity (universal/particular), quality (affirmative/negative), and whether the predicate is modified (has 'لا' prefix) or simple. Write down both propositions clearly.
The passage gives three examples: universal affirmative simple ↔ universal negative modified; particular negative simple? Actually 'not every' ↔ some modified; universal affirmative modified ↔ universal negative simple.
2
Check the three conditions for equivalence
Verify that the two propositions share the same quantity, differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and that one has a modified predicate while the other has a simple predicate. The subject term must be assumed to have existing referents ('al-mawḍūʿ fī ḥukm al-mawjūd').
If the subject is empty, the equivalence fails (e.g., 'Every human is just' and 'No human is just' are not equivalent if no humans exist).
3
Apply the conditions to an Islamic example
Take the proposition 'Every intoxicant is haram (simple predicate)'. Its equivalent modified proposition is 'No intoxicant is not haram (modified predicate)'. Check the conditions: both universal, opposite quality, one simple one modified, and intoxicants exist. So they are equivalent.
If intoxicants did not exist, 'Every intoxicant is haram' would be vacuously true, but 'No intoxicant is not haram' would also be vacuously true, but the equivalence might still hold? Actually the author requires existential import for the equivalence to be meaningful; without existents, the propositions are not considered equivalent in the intended sense.
4
Trace the proof by contradiction for the first example
Assume 'Every human exists just' is true. Then 'No human exists not just' (the universal negative with modified predicate) would be false, so its contradictory 'Some human exists not just' is true. But that implies 'Some human is not just', which contradicts the original if we also assume 'All humans are just'? Actually the proof shows that if the universal affirmative is true, the universal negative modified cannot be false, so they are equivalent.
The full proof is in the passage: if 'كل إنسان يوجد عدلا' is true, then 'لا واحد من الإنسان يوجد لا عدلا' is false, so its contradictory 'واحد من الإنسان يوجد لا عدلا' is true, leading to a contradiction with 'كل إنسان غير عدلا'? The author says 'this is a contradiction'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا شارك القضيتان في الكم واختلفا في الكيف وفي العدول والتحصيل من جهة المحمول وكان الموضوع في حكم الموجود فهما متلازمان. فإن قولنا كل إنسان يوجد عدلا يلازم قولنا لا واحد من الإنسان يوجد عدلا إذا كان الموضوع في حكم الموجود. وقولنا ليس كل إنسان يوجد عدلا يلازم قولنا بعض الإنسان يوجد لا عدلا بعد الشرائط المذكورة. وقولنا كل إنسان يوجد لا عدلا يلازم قولنا لا واحد من الإنسان يوجد عدلا. وأما البرهان عليه وليكن على المثال الأول: إنا إذا قلنا كل إنسان يوجد عدلا فقد كذب قولنا لا واحد من الإنسان يوجد لا عدلا فيصدق نقيضه وهو واحد من الإنسان يوجد لا عدلا وكان كل إنسان غير عدلا؛ هذا خلف، وأنت تعرف الحال من هذه الصورة.

But the author says they differ in quality and modifier? Actually 'عدلا' is simple, 'لا عدلا' is modified? The text says 'في العدول والتحصيل من جهة المحمول'. In the first pair, both have 'عدلا'? Possibly a miswriting. The proof uses the first example: if 'كل إنسان يوجد عدلا' is true, then 'لا واحد من الإنسان يوجد لا عدلا' is false, so its contradictory 'واحد من الإنسان يوجد لا عدلا' is true, leading to 'كل إنسان غير عدلا' which is a contradiction. So the equivalence is between a universal affirmative with simple predicate and a universal negative with modified predicate? Actually 'لا واحد من الإنسان يوجد لا عدلا' is 'No human exists not just', which is a universal negative with modified predicate. So the equivalence is: universal affirmative simple = universal negative modified. Similarly, 'ليس كل إنسان يوجد عدلا' (not all human is just) equivalent to 'بعض الإنسان يوجد لا عدلا' (some human exists not just). And 'كل إنسان يوجد لا عدلا' (every human exists not just) equivalent to 'لا واحد من الإنسان يوجد عدلا' (no human exists just). These are standard conversions of obversion and contraposition. The author provides a proof by contradiction for the first case.

Key Terms
متلازمان
الموضوع في حكم الموجود
خلف

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Modified and unmodified propositions are equivalent only when the subject term has existing referents.

Equivalence of Negative Propositions (Ma'dula): Conditions and Proof
Author defines conditions for equivalence between affirmative and negative propositions with negation on predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a universal affirmative proposition is equivalent to a universal negative proposition with a negated predicate, and apply the reductio proof to verify such equivalences.

Core Idea
Equivalence of propositions requires same quantity, opposite quality, and subject existence; proof is by reductio.

This passage introduces a key tool in immediate inference: determining when propositions with negated predicates (ma'dula) are logically equivalent. The author provides both the conditions for equivalence and a proof method. Understanding this is essential for building valid arguments in Islamic sciences, such as when establishing equivalences in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions being compared
Find the two statements that the author claims are equivalent. For example, 'Every human is just' and 'Not one human is just'.
The Arabic wording: 'كل إنسان يوجد عدلا' and 'لا واحد من الإنسان يوجد عدلا'.
2
Check quantity and quality
Ensure the two propositions share the same quantity (universal/particular) but differ in quality (affirmative/negative) and in whether the predicate is negated (udul/tahsil).
The phrase 'فإذا شارك القضيتان في الكم واختلفا في الكيف وفي العدول والتحصيل من جهة المحمول' indicates this.
3
Verify subject existence
The author adds the condition that the subject must be considered existent (الموضوع في حكم الموجود). Without this, the equivalence may fail.
This is a technical condition to avoid vacuous truth issues.
4
Apply the proof by reductio
For the first example, the author proves equivalence by assuming one proposition true and deriving a contradiction if the other were false.
Follow the steps: assume 'Every human is just', then 'Not one human is not just' is false, so its contradictory 'Some human is not just' is true, leading to contradiction with the assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معدولا فلنجعل حرف السلب جزءًا من الموضوع. فإذا شارك القضيتان في الكم واختلفا في الكيف وفي العدول والتحصيل من جهة المحمول وكان الموضوع في حكم الموجود فهما متلازمان. فإن قولنا كل إنسان يوجد عدلا يلازم قولنا لا واحد من الإنسان يوجد عدلا إذا كان الموضوع في حكم الموجود. وقولنا ليس كل إنسان يوجد عدلا يلازم قولنا بعض الإنسان يوجد لا عدلا بعد الشرائط المذكورة. وقولنا كل إنسان يوجد لا عدلا يلازم قولنا لا واحد من الإنسان يوجد عدلا . وأما البرهان عليه وليكن على المثال الأول: إنا إذا قلنا كل إنسان يوجد عدلًا فقد كذب قولنا لا واحد من الإنسان يوجد لا عدلا فيصدق نقيضه وهو واحد من الإنسان يوجد لا عدلا وكان كل إنسان جد عدلا؛ هذا خلف، وأنت تعرف الحال من هذه الصورة.
Let us make the negation particle part of the subject. If two propositions share quantity and differ in quality and in negation and affirmation with respect to the predicate, and the subject is considered existent, then they are equivalent. For our statement 'Every human is just' is equivalent to 'Not one human is just' when the subject is considered existent. And our statement 'Not every human is just' is equivalent to 'Some human is not just' after the mentioned conditions. And our statement 'Every human is not just' is equivalent to 'Not one human is just.' As for the proof of that, let it be on the first example: If we say 'Every human is just,' then our statement 'Not one human is not just' is false, so its contradictory, 'One human is not just,' is true, and every human was just; this is a contradiction. And you know the situation from this form.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions under which negative categorical propositions (المعدولة) are logically equivalent to each other. The author begins by instructing that we treat the negation particle as part of the subject term. He then states the condition for equivalence: two propositions must share the same quantity (universal/particular) but differ in quality (affirmative/negative) and in whether the predicate is negated or affirmed, assuming the subject is existent. He provides three examples: (1) 'Every human is just' is equivalent to 'Not one human is just' (i.e., 'No human is just') when subject exists. (2) 'Not every human is just' is equivalent to 'Some human is not just.' (3) 'Every human is not just' is equivalent to 'No human is just.' Then he gives a proof by reductio for the first example: Assume 'Every human is just' is true. Then the statement 'No human is not just' (i.e., there is no human that is not just) is false, because if every human is just, then there is no human that is not just. But we take the negation of that false statement to get its contradictory: 'Some human is not just.' But if every human is just, then that some human is not just is false, leading to a contradiction. The argument shows the equivalence.

Key Terms
معدولا
الكم
الكيف
العدول
التحصيل
الموضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition is 'Every S is P' equivalent to 'No S is not-P'?
A) When S and P are the same.
B) When the subject is considered existent.
C) When the predicate is affirmative.
D) When the quantity is particular.

Key Takeaway
Equivalence between affirmative and negative propositions with negated predicates requires same quantity, opposite quality, and subject existence; proof is by reductio.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a pair of equivalent propositions using the condition given in the passage. For example, start with 'Every act of worship is obligatory' and find its equivalent according to the condition. Ensure the subject is considered existent (i.e., acts of worship exist).

The Specific Possible and Types of Power
Author defines specific possible and power types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the specific possible (al-mumkin al-khass) and distinguish between active power (quwwa fa'ila) and dispositional power (quwwa isti'dadiyya) using examples from Islamic philosophy.

Core Idea
The specific possible can both exist and not exist, unlike the necessary possible.

In Islamic logic, possibility is not a single concept. The author distinguishes between what can exist and not exist (specific possible) and what can only exist (necessary possible). He uses the analogy of walking to explain active versus dispositional power. This lesson will equip you to identify the specific possible and understand the two types of power.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of the specific possible
Locate the phrase 'الممكن الخاص' and note the conditions: it can exist and can not exist. This is the contingent.
2
Contrast with necessary possible
The author says 'ليس كذلك كل ممكن' – not every possible is like this. The necessary possible can only exist, not not exist.
3
Examine the example of active vs dispositional power
Active power (quwwa fa'ila) does not admit non-existence at the moment (e.g., actually walking). Dispositional power (quwwa isti'dadiyya) admits both (e.g., ability to walk while not walking).
The actual (bi al-fi'l) applies to eternal and changing things, but potential (bi al-quwwa) only to changing things.
4
Understand the scope of actual and potential
The actual is common to both eternal and changing things, while the potential is specific to changing things. This reinforces the definition of the specific possible as contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالذي هو واجب من حيث إنه موجود لا يكون دائم الوجود والذي هو ممتنع من حيث إنه معدوم لا يكون دائم العدم. ولو كان كذلك فلا يستحيل أن يصير الموجود معدوما والمعدوم موجودًا: فلا يكون وجوده ضروريًا ولا عدمه كذلك ؛ وهذا هو الممكن الخاص يصدق فيه ممكن أن يوجد وممكن أن لا يوجد. وليس كذلك كل ممكن فإنه إذا كان ضروريًا يصدق فيه ممكن أن يوجد ولا يصدق ممكن أن لا يوجد. ألا ترى أن القوة إذا كانت فاعلة فلا يصدق فيه ممكن أن لا يوجد، وإذا كانت استعدادية يصدق فيه ممكن أن يوجد وممكن أن لا يوجد؛ فيقال على الذي يمشي وهو يمشي؛ وإنه مما يقال على الفعل، وعلى الذي يقوى أن يمشي وهو لا يمشي؛ وإنه مما يقال على القوة. والذي بالفعل يشترك فيه الأزليات والمتغيرات والذي بالقوة لا يشترك بل يختص بالمتغيرات. وقد علمت بأن الكلي محمول على كل واحد من الجزئيات فالممكن إذن محمول على الواجبات وعلى غير الواجبات فيكون أعم من الواجبات لا محالة. ولا مجال للعمومية والخصوصية بدون الملازمة فإن ما من خاص إلا ويلزم من وجوده وجود العام، وما من عام إلا ويلزم من عدمه عدم الخاص. وكيف لا يلزم والخاص عبارة عما يستلزم غيره من غير عكس، والعام عبارة عما يستلزم الغير من غير عكس؛ وهذه الملازمات من الملازمات اللا متعاكسة لما أنه يلزم من وجود هذا وجود ذلك ولا ينعكس. وكما أنه يلزم من وجود الخاص وجود العام فكذلك يلزم من وجوده ما يجعله خاصًا بالنسبة إلى ذلك؛ وذلك مما يساويه في الوجود فيلزم من وجود ذلك وجوده أيضًا : وهذه الملازمات متعاكسة. فقولنا واجب أن يوجد يلزمه، وينعكس عليه ممتنع أن لا يوجد وليس يمكن العامي أن لا يوجد، وهذه هي طبقات هذه الملازمات:
That which is necessary insofar as it exists is not eternal existence, and that which is impossible insofar as it is non-existent is not eternal non-existence. If it were so, it would not be impossible for the existent to become non-existent and the non-existent to become existent. So its existence is not necessary nor its non-existence necessary. This is the specific possible (al-mumkin al-khass), of which it is true that it is possible to exist and possible not to exist. Not every possible is like this; if it is necessary, it is true that it is possible to exist but not true that it is possible not to exist. Do you not see that if the power (quwwa) is active (fa'ila), it is not true that it is possible for it not to exist, but if it is potential/dispositional (isti'dadiyya), it is true that it is possible to exist and possible not to exist? So it is said of the one who is walking while he is walking — that is said of the act; and of the one who has the power to walk but is not walking — that is said of the power. The actual (bi al-fi'l) is common to eternal things and changing things, while the potential (bi al-quwwa) is not common but is specific to changing things. And you have known that the universal is predicated of every one of the particulars. So 'possible' is predicated of the necessary and of other than the necessary, thus it is broader than the necessary without doubt. And there is no room for generality and specificity without implication (luzum). For there is no particular except that its existence implies the existence of the universal, and no universal except that its non-existence implies the non-existence of the particular. How could it not imply, while the particular is that which implies something else without reciprocity, and the universal is that which implies something else without reciprocity? These implications are non-reciprocal (ghayr muta'akisa) because the existence of this implies the existence of that but not vice versa. And just as the existence of the particular implies the existence of the universal, so too the existence of what makes it particular relative to that implies that; that thing is equal to it in existence, so the existence of that implies its existence as well. These implications are reciprocal. So our statement 'necessary to exist' is implied by it, and reciprocally implied by 'impossible not to exist', and it is not possible for the general [possible?] not to exist. And these are the levels of these implications.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage explains the specific possible (al-mumkin al-khass) and extends the discussion to the relationship between universal and particular. The author first clarifies that the specific possible is that which can both exist and not exist, as opposed to the necessary possible which can only exist. He illustrates this with a distinction between active power (quwwa fa'ila) and dispositional power (quwwa isti'dadiyya): active power, like actually walking, does not admit non-existence at that moment; dispositional power, like the ability to walk while not walking, admits both. The actual (bi al-fi'l) applies to both eternal and changing things, but the potential is only for changing things. Then the author shifts to the universal and particular. Since 'possible' is a universal term predicated of both necessary and contingent things, it is broader than 'necessary'. The relationship between universal and particular involves implication (luzum). The existence of a particular implies the existence of its universal (e.g., the existence of a man implies the existence of animal), but not vice versa — this is non-reciprocal implication. Similarly, the non-existence of a universal implies the non-existence of its particular. However, when the particular and something else are equal in existence, the implication can be reciprocal. For example, 'necessary to exist' and 'impossible not to exist' mutually imply each other. The passage concludes by mentioning levels of these implications.

Key Terms
ممكن خاص
قوة فاعلة
قوة استعدادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true of the specific possible?
A) It is necessary in existence.
B) It can be non-existent.
C) It is eternal.
D) It only applies to the actual.

Key Takeaway
The specific possible is defined as that which admits both existence and non-existence, distinguishing it from the necessary possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Give a concrete example from Islamic theology of a contingent thing (e.g., the world) and explain why it fits the definition of specific possible: it can exist and not exist.
?
Distinguish between active power (quwwa fa'ila) and dispositional power (quwwa isti'dadiyya) using a fard 'ayn example: your ability to pray now (active) versus your ability to learn Arabic (dispositional).

Universal-Particular Implication: Reciprocal and Non-Reciprocal
Author explains universal-particular implication types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between reciprocal and non-reciprocal implication in the context of universal and particular, and identify examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The existence of a particular always implies the universal, but not vice versa.

The author extends the discussion to the logical relationship between universals and particulars. He explains that every particular implies its universal (non-reciprocally), but when two terms are equivalent in existence, the implication becomes reciprocal. This lesson will help you master these logical relationships used in definition and argument in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recap the universal-particular relationship
The author states that 'possible' is a universal term predicated of both necessary and contingent things, so it is broader than 'necessary'. This sets the stage for discussing implication.
2
Identify the two types of implication
Look for 'ملازمة غير متعاكسة' (non-reciprocal) and 'ملازمة متعاكسة' (reciprocal). The condition for non-reciprocal: existence of particular implies universal, but not reverse. For reciprocal: two terms are equal in existence, so each implies the other.
3
Apply the non-reciprocal rule
Example: The existence of a human (particular) implies the existence of animal (universal). But animal does not imply human. This is because the particular is more specific.
The author says: 'ما من خاص إلا ويلزم من وجوده وجود العام' – every particular's existence implies the universal's existence.
4
Apply the reciprocal rule
Terms that are equal in existence, like 'necessary to exist' and 'impossible not to exist', imply each other. They are co-extensive.
The author gives the example: 'واجب أن يوجد' implies 'ممتنع أن لا يوجد' and vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالذي هو واجب من حيث إنه موجود لا يكون دائم الوجود والذي هو ممتنع من حيث إنه معدوم لا يكون دائم العدم. ولو كان كذلك فلا يستحيل أن يصير الموجود معدوما والمعدوم موجودًا: فلا يكون وجوده ضروريًا ولا عدمه كذلك ؛ وهذا هو الممكن الخاص يصدق فيه ممكن أن يوجد وممكن أن لا يوجد. وليس كذلك كل ممكن فإنه إذا كان ضروريًا يصدق فيه ممكن أن يوجد ولا يصدق ممكن أن لا يوجد. ألا ترى أن القوة إذا كانت فاعلة فلا يصدق فيه ممكن أن لا يوجد، وإذا كانت استعدادية يصدق فيه ممكن أن يوجد وممكن أن لا يوجد؛ فيقال على الذي يمشي وهو يمشي؛ وإنه مما يقال على الفعل، وعلى الذي يقوى أن يمشي وهو لا يمشي؛ وإنه مما يقال على القوة. والذي بالفعل يشترك فيه الأزليات والمتغيرات والذي بالقوة لا يشترك بل يختص بالمتغيرات. وقد علمت بأن الكلي محمول على كل واحد من الجزئيات فالممكن إذن محمول على الواجبات وعلى غير الواجبات فيكون أعم من الواجبات لا محالة. ولا مجال للعمومية والخصوصية بدون الملازمة فإن ما من خاص إلا ويلزم من وجوده وجود العام، وما من عام إلا ويلزم من عدمه عدم الخاص. وكيف لا يلزم والخاص عبارة عما يستلزم غيره من غير عكس، والعام عبارة عما يستلزم الغير من غير عكس؛ وهذه الملازمات من الملازمات اللا متعاكسة لما أنه يلزم من وجود هذا وجود ذلك ولا ينعكس. وكما أنه يلزم من وجود الخاص وجود العام فكذلك يلزم من وجوده ما يجعله خاصًا بالنسبة إلى ذلك؛ وذلك مما يساويه في الوجود فيلزم من وجود ذلك وجوده أيضًا : وهذه الملازمات متعاكسة. فقولنا واجب أن يوجد يلزمه، وينعكس عليه ممتنع أن لا يوجد وليس يمكن العامي أن لا يوجد، وهذه هي طبقات هذه الملازمات:

Modal Layers and Their Entailments
Author enumerates modal layers and their entailments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six modal layers and list at least two entailments for each layer from memory.

Core Idea
Modal propositions form a network of entailments bound by logical equivalence.

The author systematically enumerates the fundamental modal categories—necessity, impossibility, and possibility—along with their negations. For each, he specifies what other modal propositions are entailed. This network of immediate inferences is essential for understanding how modal statements relate in kalam arguments about divine attributes or in usul al-fiqh concerning obligation and prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six modal layers
Extract the six layers: wajib an yujad, wajib an la yujad, not necessary to exist, not necessary not to exist, possible to exist, not possible to exist. Note that the passage explicitly lists five but implies six.
The author states there are three positive (wajibat) and three negative (salibat).
2
Map the entailments for each layer
For each layer, write down the modal propositions that are entailed. For example, 'necessary not to exist' entails 'not impossible not to exist' and 'common possibility not to exist'.
Use the lists given in the Arabic.
3
Classify layers as positive or negative
Identify which layers are positive (affirmative modality) and which are negative (negated modality).
The positive layers are wajib an yujad, wajib an la yujad, mumkin an yujad; negatives are their negations.
4
Observe the role of the contradictory
Note that the contradictory of each layer contains the other two, meaning the contradictory is the most general entailment.
This shows the logical hierarchy.

[ب] وأما طبقة «ليس بواجب أن يوجد»، فلا يلزمها شيء غيرهما ينعكس عليها
[ت] وأما طبقة واجب أن لا يوجد»، فيلزمها:
ليس بممتنع أن لا يوجد
ليس بممتنع
ممكن العامي أن لا يوجد
ليس بممكن الخاصي أن يوجد
ليس بممكن الخاصي أن لا يوجد
[ث] وأما طبقة ليس بواجب أن لا يوجد»، فلا يلزمها شيء غير ما ينعكس عليها
[ج] وأما طبقة «ممكن أن يوجد» فيلزمها:
ليس بواجب أن يوجد
ليس بواجب أن لا يوجد
ليس بممتنع أن يوجد
ليس بممتنع أن لا يوجد
ممكن العامي أن يوجد
ممكن العامي أن لا يوجد
[و] وأما طبقة ليس بممكن أن يوجد ، فلا يلزمها الانعكاس.
وأما الطبقات فثلاث منها واجبات متعاكسات، وثلاث منها سالبات متعاكسات، والنقيض في كل طبقة منها يحتوي على الطبقتين الباقيتين؛ فيكون هو الأعم من اللوازم بالنسبة إلى كل واحدة منهما. وكذا الممكن العامي من اللوازم محصلا للأولى ومعدولا للثانية، ومعدولا ومحصلا للثانية.
ثم لقائل أن يقول إن قولنا واجب أن يوجد لا يلزمه قولنا ممتنع أن لا يوجد إلا وأن يكون مغايرا له في المعنى فإن من المحال أن يكون الشيء لازما لنفسه. وكذلك لا يلزمه قولنا ليس بممكن العامي أن لا يوجد لكنا نقول لا يلزم أن يكون اللازم يغاير ملزومه بجميع الوجوه بل يلزم أن يغايره بوجه ما. وذلك متحقق فيما نحن فيه وإلا لكان هذا عين ذلك / فيكون نقيضه عين نقيض ذلك أيضًا؛ وليس كذلك لما مر . ثم الجواب المشهور عنه في الأول أن يقال لما كان الوجوب مغايرا للامتناع لا يمكن أن يكون هذا عين ذلك بل يكون مغايرا له ؛ وذلك ظاهر فإن الوجوب مما يتأكد به الوجود دون العدم والامتناع على العكس. وفي الثاني أن هذا لا يكون عين ذلك إلا وأن يكون الوجوب عبارة عن سلب ضرورة العدم، أما إذا كان عبارة عما يستلزم ذلك السلب من الأمور الوجودية فلا؛ وفي هذا الكلام نظر فإن الوجوب لا يكون من الأمور الوجودية على مذهب الفلاسفة، وعليه قوم من غيرهم كذلك.
[B] As for the layer 'not necessary to exist', nothing is entailed by it other than what reflects back onto it. [T] As for the layer 'necessary not to exist', it entails: not impossible not to exist; not impossible; possible in the common sense not to exist; not possible in the special sense to exist; not possible in the special sense not to exist. [Th] As for the layer 'not necessary not to exist', nothing is entailed by it other than what reflects back onto it. [J] As for the layer 'possible to exist', it entails: not necessary to exist; not necessary not to exist; not impossible to exist; not impossible not to exist; possible in the common sense to exist; possible in the common sense not to exist. [W] As for the layer 'not possible to exist', it does not entail any reflection. And the layers: three of them are positive and are reflected, and three are negative and are reflected. The contradictory in each layer encompasses the other two layers, so it is the most general of the entailments with respect to each of them. Likewise, common possibility from the entailments is affirmative for the first and negated for the second, and negated and affirmative for the second. Then someone may object: Our statement 'necessary to exist' does not entail our statement 'impossible not to exist' unless it is different in meaning from it, because it is impossible for a thing to be self-entailing. Similarly, it does not entail our statement 'not possible in the common sense not to exist'. But we say: It is not necessary that the entailing thing differs from the thing it entails in all respects, rather it must differ in some respect. This is realized in our case; otherwise, this would be identical with that, and its contradictory would be identical with the contradictory of that as well—and that is not the case, as previously mentioned. Then the well-known answer to the first objection is to say: Since necessity is different from impossibility, it cannot be that this is identical with that; rather, it is different from it. That is apparent, for necessity strengthens existence without non-existence, while impossibility is the opposite. As for the second [objection], this would be identical with that only if necessity were an expression of the negation of the necessity of non-existence. But if it is an expression of what entails that negation among existential matters, then no. And in this statement there is consideration, for necessity is not among existential matters according to the philosophers, and many others also follow them.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage defines the modal categories of necessity, impossibility, and possibility, along with their mutual entailments. The author lists five layers: (1) not necessary to exist, (2) necessary not to exist (impossible), (3) not necessary not to exist, (4) possible to exist, and (5) not possible to exist (impossible to exist?). For each, he specifies which other modal propositions are entailed. For example, 'necessary not to exist' entails 'not impossible not to exist', 'possible in the common sense not to exist', and negations of special possibilities. The author then classifies the layers into three positive (wajibat) and three negative (salibat), noting that the contradictory of each layer contains the other two. This shows the interrelation. Then an objection is raised: How can 'necessary to exist' entail 'impossible not to exist' if they are different in meaning? Self-entailment is impossible. The author responds by distinguishing between complete identity and difference in some respect; they differ in meaning (necessity vs. impossibility) but are logically equivalent. The well-known answer clarifies that necessity is a positive attribute strengthening existence, while impossibility is a negative attribute. However, the author notes a problem: philosophers deny that necessity is an existential thing, thus the answer may not hold on their view. This lesson teaches the student to carefully analyze the relationship between modal concepts and to recognize that logical equivalence does not imply synonymy.

ممكن العامي
ممكن الخاصي

D) ليس بممتنع أن يوجد (not impossible to exist)

Key Takeaway
Modal entailments define a precise logical hierarchy: necessity, impossibility, and possibility are distinct modalities that imply each other in specific ways.

Objection and Answer: Distinguishing Necessity from Impossibility
Author addresses an objection about the distinctness of necessity and impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'necessary to exist' entails 'impossible not to exist' despite them being different in meaning.

Core Idea
Logical equivalence does not imply synonymy; modal terms differ in connotation and direction.

The author presents a potential objection: if 'necessary to exist' entails 'impossible not to exist', then either they are identical (impossible for self-entailment) or the entailment fails. He responds by distinguishing the two concepts: necessity is a positive attribute of existence, impossibility is a negative attribute of non-existence. Thus they are logically equivalent but distinct in meaning. This clarifies a key nuance in modal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objector argues that if 'necessary to exist' entails 'impossible not to exist', then they must be different in meaning because self-entailment is impossible. Are they different? The objector implies they must be.
The objection is not a rejection but a demand for distinction.
2
Analyze the author's response
The author accepts they are different. He says difference in some respect suffices; necessity strengthens existence, impossibility strengthens non-existence.
The well-known answer distinguishes them as positive vs. negative.
3
Evaluate the philosophical nuance
The author notes a problem: if necessity is not an existential thing (on the philosophers' view), then the response may not hold. This shows the deep metaphysical assumptions.
This nuance is important for understanding the limitations of the response.

The Contradictory Negative is More Opposed than the Contrary
Author argues that the contradictory negative is more opposed than the contrary affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between contradictory and contrary opposition and explain why the contradictory is more opposed in judgment.

Core Idea
The contradictory negative directly denies the predicate, making it more opposed than any contrary affirmative.

In this passage, the author investigates whether a contrary affirmative (e.g., 'unjust') or a contradictory negative (e.g., 'not just') is more opposed to an affirmative proposition like 'Zayd is just'. He argues from the nature of judgment and belief that the contradictory is more severe because it can never agree in truth or falsehood, whereas the contrary can both be false.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of opposition
Recognize that the author distinguishes between contrariety (tadādd) and contradiction (tanāquḍ) as forms of ta'ānud.
2
Focus on the example 'Zayd is just'
The author asks which is more opposed: 'Zayd is unjust' (contrary) or 'Zayd is not just' (contradictory).
3
Understand the condition of subject identity
The author stresses that genuine opposition requires the same subject; otherwise, beliefs can coexist.
4
Trace the argument for the negative
The contradictory negative ('not just') directly negates the predicate, and thus can never be true or false in agreement with the affirmative, making it the stronger opponent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم البحث في هذا الأمر لا يكون من الأبحاث المنطقية بل من الجدلية لكن جرت العادة من السلف أن يتعرضوا لهذا البحث في هذا الموضع لما له من النسبة على ما تقدم فنتعرض له إذن ونقول : إذا قلنا زيد عادل مثلا؛ فهل قولنا زيد جائر أشد عنادًا أم قولنا ليس بعادل ؟ وهل الضد لقولنا كل إنسان عادل قولنا كل إنسان جائر أو ما سلف ذكره وهو قولنا لا واحد من الإنسان بعادل؟ فإن هذا شيء قد يتشاجر فيه طوائف والحق في هذا أن الضد لقولنا كل إنسان عادل هو قولنا كل إنسان جائر وما سلف ذكره : فذلك مما يشابه الضد من حيث إنهما لا يجتمعان ولكن يرتفعان في مادة الإمكان، وفي ذلك أن كونه جائرًا أشد عنادًا في الطبيعة لكونه عادلا من كونه ليس بجائر. أما من حيث الحكم لفظًا كان أو اعتقادًا فالسالبة أشد وأبعد من أن تطابق الموجبة في شيء من الصدق والكذب. ولما كان النظر فيه من حيث الحكم والحكم بحسب الاعتقاد في الحقيقة لما أن القول مانع للاعتقاد؛ فلننظر في هذه المعاندات من حيث الاعتقاد وليكن ذلك في أنه خير أو أنه شر أو أنه ليس بخير. / والتعاند لا بد وأن يكون بالنسبة إلى موضوع واحد فإن اعتقاد الضدين في حق موسى وفرعون مثلا أن موسى صلوات الله عليه خير، وفرعون شر؛ لا يوجب التعاند بين الاعتقاد أصلا. وكذلك اعتقاد النقيضين وهو أن موسى صلوات الله عليه خير، وفرعون ليس بخير. وكيف يوجب وقد أمكن الاجتماع بينهما على خلاف ما إذا كانا في موضوع واحد وهو أن هذا خير وشر أو خير وليس بخير. فلو لم يكن الشر ليس بخير فلا يستحيل اعتقاد أنه خير وأنه شر ولو كان بدل الشر ما ليس بخير، وليس بشر لكان مع ذلك يستحيل اعتقاد أنه خير، وليس بخیر؛ فيكون العناد بينهما لا لكونهما متضادين بل لكونهما متناقضين؛ إذ السلب مما يناقض الإيجاب بالضرورة: وذلك يعرف من بعد فتكون السالبة أشد عنادًا من الموجبة. ولأن قولنا ليس بخير مما يعاده بنفسه على خلاف قولنا هو شر فإنه لا يعاده بنفسه بل بعرضيته أنه يعاند لما يلازمه وهو أنه ليس بشر فتكون معاندته أقدم. ولو كانت معاندته أقدم فعناده أشد فتكون السالبة أشد عنادًا. ثم التضاد في الأمور لا يوجب التضاد في الاعتقاد بل يجب أن تكون متنافية حتى تكون متضادة في الاعتقاد. فلو قلنا للخير إنه خير صدقناه. ولو قلنا إنه ليس بشر فكذلك لكن الصدق في أنه خير بما هو في ذاته؛ إذ الخير خير لذاته على خلاف الصدق في أنه ليس بشر. ألا ترى أن ما يقابله وهو الشر فإنه مباين لذاته فإثبات الخير إذن يتم عليه بذاته وسلب الشر عنه بغيره وبإزائهما كذبان أيضًا مثل قولنا إنه ليس بخير؛ وهو مقابل في ذاته، وإنه شر كذلك؛ وهو مقابل الأمر عارض له والكذب في أمر ذاتي أشد عنادًا بالنسبة إلى ما يكون في أمر عرضي. وأما الظن بأن هذا أشد كذبًا بالنسبة إلى ذلك : فذلك باطل فإنه لا صدق أشد صدقًا من صدق ولا كذب أشد كذبًا من كذب بل بعض الصدق يكون أدوم وبعضه لا يكون أدوم وبعضه في أمر ذاتي، وبعضه ليس في أمر ذاتي وما يكون في أمر ذاتي فإنه أشد عنادًا. ومن جملة ما يدل عليه أيضًا هو أن يقال لا قضية إلا ولها مقابل بل من باب التناقض على خلاف ما يكون مقابلا لها من باب التضاد؛ إذ منها ما لا يقابلها شيء من هذا الباب. فلو قلنا كذا مثلث مثلا وجدنا بإزائه أنه ليس بمثلث ولم نجد كذا الذي هو ضد المثلث. ولو كان كذلك لكان المعاند هو السالبة دون الموجبة. وعناد السالبة للموجبة من حيث هي موجبة كذلك على خلاف عناد الغير. فإن ذلك من حيث هي موجبة بل لأمر عارض. لكن لقائل أن يقول ليس كلامنا في أن كل موجبة هل يعاندها موجبة أم لا؟ بل يسلم عناد السلب
Then the investigation into this matter is not from logical investigations but from dialectical ones, yet the custom of the predecessors is to address this investigation in this place because of its relation to what has preceded, so we address it now and say: If we say 'Zayd is just' for example, is our saying 'Zayd is unjust' more opposed, or our saying 'He is not just'? And is the contrary to our saying 'Every human is just' our saying 'Every human is unjust' or what was previously mentioned, namely our saying 'No human is just'? For this is something about which groups dispute. The truth in this is that the contrary to our saying 'Every human is just' is our saying 'Every human is unjust', and what was previously mentioned [the negative] resembles the contrary in that they do not coexist, but they both can be false in possible matter. In that, being unjust is more opposed in nature to being just than being not unjust is. But from the perspective of judgment, whether verbal or belief, the negative is more severe and further from agreeing with the affirmative in any truth or falsehood. Since the consideration is from the perspective of judgment, and judgment according to belief in reality because speech prevents belief, let us consider these oppositions from the perspective of belief, and let that be regarding 'He is good', 'He is evil', or 'He is not good'. Opposition must be with respect to a single subject. For belief in two contraries regarding Moses and Pharaoh, for example, that Moses (peace be upon him) is good and Pharaoh is evil, does not necessitate opposition between the beliefs at all. Similarly, belief in two contradictories: that Moses is good and Pharaoh is not good. How would it necessitate, when they can coexist, unlike the case when they are in one subject: that this is good and evil, or good and not good? If 'evil' were not 'not good', then it would not be impossible to believe that it is good and that it is evil. And if instead of 'evil' we had 'not good' and 'not evil', still it would be impossible to believe that it is good and not good; so the opposition between them would not be because they are contraries but because they are contradictories, since negation necessarily contradicts affirmation. This will be known later. Thus the negative is more opposed than the affirmative. And because our saying 'not good' opposes it by itself, unlike our saying 'it is evil', for it does not oppose it by itself but accidentally, because it opposes what is concomitant with it, namely 'it is not evil', so its opposition is more primary. If its opposition is more primary, then its opposition is stronger, so the negative is more opposed. Then contrariety in things does not necessitate contrariety in belief; rather they must be mutually exclusive to be contrary in belief. So if we say of the good that it is good, we affirm it as true. And if we say that it is not evil, similarly, but the truth in 'it is good' is by its essence, for the good is good by itself, contrary to the truth in 'it is not evil'. Do you not see that its opposite, which is evil, is distinct from it by essence, so the affirmation of good is established by its essence, and the negation of evil from it by other than it. Opposite to both are also falsehoods, like our saying 'it is not good', which is opposite by essence, and 'it is evil', which is opposite accidentally. Falsehood in an essential matter is more opposed relative to that which is in an accidental matter. As for the opinion that this is more false relative to that, that is false, for there is no truth truer than truth, nor falsehood falser than falsehood. Rather, some truth is more lasting and some is not, and some is in an essential matter, and some is not in an essential matter. What is in an essential matter is more opposed. Among what indicates this as well is that it is said: There is no proposition except that it has a counterpart in the way of contradiction, unlike what is its counterpart in the way of contrariety, for some propositions have no counterpart from this way. For example, if we say 'this is triangular', we find opposite it 'it is not triangular', and we do not find something that is the contrary of triangular. If that were the case, then the opposing proposition would be the negative, not the affirmative. And the opposition of the negative to the affirmative as such is different from the opposition of something else. For that opposition is as such, but rather for an accidental reason. But someone might say: Our discussion is not about whether every affirmative is opposed by an affirmative or not? Rather we concede the opposition of the negative.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological investigation into the nature of opposition (ta'ānud) between propositions, specifically comparing the contradictory negative (saliba) with the contrary affirmative (ḍidd). The author begins by noting that this investigation is more dialectical than purely logical, but follows the tradition of addressing it here due to its relevance. The central question: Is 'Zayd is unjust' more opposed to 'Zayd is just', or is 'Zayd is not just'? Similarly for universal propositions: is the contrary of 'Every human is just' 'Every human is unjust' or 'No human is just'? The author states that the truth is that the contrary (every human is unjust) is opposite in the sense that they cannot be simultaneously true, but both can be false (in possible matter). However, in terms of judgment (ḥukm)—whether verbal or belief—the negative (saliba) is more severe in opposition because it can never agree with the affirmative in truth or falsehood. The investigation then focuses on belief (i'tiqād) as the basis of judgment. The author stresses that genuine opposition requires the same subject. For example, believing that Moses is good and Pharaoh is evil does not create opposition because the subjects differ. Similarly, believing that Moses is good and Pharaoh is not good also does not create opposition because they can coexist in belief (since they are about different subjects). Only when the subject is identical, such as 'this is good' and 'this is evil', does opposition arise. If 'evil' were not equivalent to 'not good', then believing that something is both good and evil would be possible (since they could be non-contradictory). But since 'evil' implies 'not good', the true opposition is between 'good' and 'not good' (contradictories), not between 'good' and 'evil' (contraries). Hence the negative (e.g., 'not good') is more opposed because it directly contradicts the affirmative. The author explains that 'not good' opposes by itself, whereas 'evil' opposes accidentally because it implies 'not good'. Thus the contradictory is more primary and stronger in opposition. Further, contrariety in extra-mental things does not automatically transfer to belief; for two beliefs to be contrary, the objects must be mutually exclusive. For instance, the truth of 'it is good' is essential (good by itself), while 'it is not evil' is true accidentally (because evil is distinct from good). Falsehoods also differ: 'it is not good' is false essentially, while 'it is evil' is false accidentally. Essential falsehood is more opposed. The author rejects the notion that one falsehood can be 'more false' than another—falsehood is absolute—but acknowledges that some falsehoods are more fundamental because they pertain to essence. Finally, the author argues that every proposition has a contradictory counterpart (via negation), but not every proposition has a contrary. For example, 'this is triangular' has no contrary (there is no shape that is the opposite of triangularity in the way contraries exist). Thus the contradictory negative is always the universal opponent. The passage ends with an objection: someone might say the discussion is not about whether every affirmative has an opposing affirmative, but rather the negative is conceded as opponent. This objection remains unanswered in the provided text.

Essential vs Accidental Opposition and the Universality of Contradiction
Further arguments on essential vs accidental opposition and the universality of contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an opposing proposition is essentially or accidentally opposed, and determine if a proposition has a contrary.

Core Idea
The contradictory negative opposes essentially, while the contrary opposes accidentally; moreover, every proposition has a contradictory but not every proposition has a contrary.

In this continuation, the author deepens the argument by distinguishing essential opposition (like 'not good' opposing 'good') from accidental opposition (like 'evil' opposing 'good' via implication). He also introduces the triangular example to show that some propositions lack a contrary, making the contradictory universally applicable. The passage ends with an objection indicating the debate continues.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish essential from accidental opposition
Note the author's claim that 'not good' opposes by itself, while 'evil' opposes only because it implies 'not good'.
2
Consider truth and falsehood in essential vs accidental matters
The author argues that falsehood in an essential matter (e.g., 'it is not good' when it is good) is more opposed than accidental falsehood.
3
Examine the triangular example
'This is triangular' has no contrary; its only counterpart via opposition is the contradictory 'This is not triangular'. This shows the universality of contradiction.
4
Analyze the objection
The objector says the discussion is about whether every affirmative has an opposing affirmative, but concedes the negative's opposition. Consider how this fits the argument.
