The Essence Condition for Contradiction
Condition for negation to be contradiction based on essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given negation is a genuine contradiction by checking if it negates an essential attribute.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires negation of the essence, not an accident.

In logic, not every negation produces a contradiction. A negation that denies an accidental property of a subject does not contradict the original affirmative statement. As the author explains, only negation based on the essence (dhāt) yields true contradiction. This distinction is crucial for precise reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Look at the subject and predicate to determine what is being affirmed.
2
Examine the negation
See what exactly is being negated: is it the same predicate or a different one?
3
Check if the negation is based on essence
Determine whether the predicate is an essential attribute (dhātī) of the subject. If yes, the negation is contradictory. If the negation pertains to something accidental, it is not a contradiction.
4
Apply to a classical example
Test on a proposition from kalam or usul, such as 'Every intoxicant is haram' versus 'No intoxicant is haram'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: لا يكون السلب من باب التناقض إلا وأن يكون بحسب الذات لأنه إذا كان بحسب الغير فلا يكون من هذا الباب على ما ستعرفه من بعد. وما قيل في أن ما يضاد قولنا كل إنسان خير هو قولنا كل إنسان ليس بخير أو لا واحد من الإنسان بخير أو كل إنسان شر أو لا واحد من الإنسان بلا شر : فذلك يفتقر إلى البحث في التضاد بأنه ما هو وكيف هو. فإنه إذا عُرف عُرف في سائر الشخصيات والكليات. وأما المهملات فلا يجري فيها التضاد وقد يصدق معا؛ وكذلك الجزئيات.
We say: Negation is only from the category of contradiction if it is according to the essence, because if it is according to something else, it is not from this category, as you will know later. And what is said that what opposes our statement 'Every human is good' is our statement 'Every human is not good' or 'No human is good' or 'Every human is evil' or 'No human is without evil': that requires investigation into opposition (tadadd) as to what it is and how it is. For if it is known, it is known in all particulars and universals. As for indefinite propositions (muhmalaat), opposition does not apply to them and they may both be true together; likewise particular propositions.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage defines the condition for a negation to count as a contradiction (tanaqud). The author states that a contradictory negation must be based on the essence (dhat) of the subject, not on accidental attributes. For example, if someone says 'Every human is good,' the contradictory is 'Every human is not good' or 'No human is good,' but if the negation pertains to something non-essential, it is not a true contradiction. The author then clarifies that opposition (tadadd) between universal affirmative and universal negative requires investigation, and that indefinite and particular propositions do not have true opposition because they can both be true simultaneously (e.g., 'Some human is good' and 'Some human is not good' are not contradictory).

سلب
ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'Every intoxicant is not haram'
B) 'Some intoxicant is haram'
C) 'No intoxicant is haram'
D) 'Every intoxicant is halal'

Key Takeaway
A negation is a contradiction only if it denies an essential attribute of the subject.

?

?

Understanding Opposition (Tadadd) and Its Limits
Opposition defined and limited to universal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which types of propositions admit opposition and explain why indefinite and particular propositions do not.

Core Idea
Opposition requires universal propositions; indefinite and particular can both be true.

The author introduces the concept of opposition (tadadd), which is a relation between two universal propositions that cannot both be true. However, not all propositions exhibit opposition. Indefinite propositions (muhmalaat) and particular propositions (juz'iyyat) can be true simultaneously, so they do not fall under opposition.

Guided Path
1
Recall universal affirmative and negative propositions
Understand the forms such as 'Every S is P' and 'No S is P'.
2
Define opposition
Two propositions are opposed if they cannot both be true. In classical logic, 'Every S is P' and 'No S is P' are in opposition (contraries).
3
Check indefinite and particular propositions
Indefinite (no quantifier) and particular ('Some S is P') can both be true together. For example, 'Human is good' and 'Human is not good' can both be true if some humans are good and some are not.
4
Conclude scope of opposition
Opposition applies only to universal propositions (and not all of them, as the author notes it requires investigation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: لا يكون السلب من باب التناقض إلا وأن يكون بحسب الذات لأنه إذا كان بحسب الغير فلا يكون من هذا الباب على ما ستعرفه من بعد. وما قيل في أن ما يضاد قولنا كل إنسان خير هو قولنا كل إنسان ليس بخير أو لا واحد من الإنسان بخير أو كل إنسان شر أو لا واحد من الإنسان بلا شر : فذلك يفتقر إلى البحث في التضاد بأنه ما هو وكيف هو. فإنه إذا عُرف عُرف في سائر الشخصيات والكليات. وأما المهملات فلا يجري فيها التضاد وقد يصدق معا؛ وكذلك الجزئيات.
We say: Negation is only from the category of contradiction if it is according to the essence, because if it is according to something else, it is not from this category, as you will know later. And what is said that what opposes our statement 'Every human is good' is our statement 'Every human is not good' or 'No human is good' or 'Every human is evil' or 'No human is without evil': that requires investigation into opposition (tadadd) as to what it is and how it is. For if it is known, it is known in all particulars and universals. As for indefinite propositions (muhmalaat), opposition does not apply to them and they may both be true together; likewise particular propositions.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage defines the condition for a negation to count as a contradiction (tanaqud). The author states that a contradictory negation must be based on the essence (dhat) of the subject, not on accidental attributes. For example, if someone says 'Every human is good,' the contradictory is 'Every human is not good' or 'No human is good,' but if the negation pertains to something non-essential, it is not a true contradiction. The author then clarifies that opposition (tadadd) between universal affirmative and universal negative requires investigation, and that indefinite and particular propositions do not have true opposition because they can both be true simultaneously (e.g., 'Some human is good' and 'Some human is not good' are not contradictory).

Types of Permanence Propositions Based on the Subject
Author classifies propositions of permanence based on condition from the subject side

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish al-da'ima al-mutlaqa, al-'urfiyya al-'amma, and al-'urfiyya al-khassa based on the condition from the subject side.

Core Idea
Permanence in propositions is not always absolute; it can be conditioned by the subject's essence or description.

In Islamic logic, propositions are not only evaluated for truth but also for the duration of that truth. This passage provides a rigorous classification of permanence (al-dawam) into absolute and conditional types, focusing on conditions originating from the subject. Mastering this taxonomy is essential for correctly analyzing modal propositions in kalam, fiqh, and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division
The author divides permanence into absolute (mutlaq) and conditional (mashrut). Locate the sentence that introduces this division.
The second type is conditional upon a condition, which may be from the subject, predicate, or neither.
2
Trace the condition source
The passage elaborates on conditions from the subject side. Find the two cases marked [أ] and [ب].
Case [أ] is based on the subject's essence; case [ب] is based on a description of the subject.
3
Analyze the first case (essence-based)
The absolute perpetual (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa) holds for the entire duration of the subject's existence. Example: 'Every human is an animal permanently.' Think of a similar example from fiqh or kalam.
No extra condition is needed; the predicate is true as long as the subject exists.
4
Analyze the second case (description-based)
Here permanence is tied to a description of the subject. If the description is perpetual (coextensive with the essence), it collapses into the absolute perpetual. If not, it splits into two subcases.
Pay attention to the subcases: generic 'urfiyya (al-'urfiyya al-'amma) and specific 'urfiyya (al-'urfiyya al-khassa).
5
Distinguish generic vs. specific 'urfiyya
The generic 'urfiyya conditions permanence only on the description (e.g., 'as long as it is wine'), without adding non-permanence. The specific 'urfiyya explicitly includes non-permanence (e.g., 'not permanently but as long as it is wine'). Note that the generic 'urfiyya is also called 'absolute converse' because its negative converts.
This conversion property is used in immediate inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدوام فإنه قد يكون مطلقا وقد لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط؛ وذلك الشرط إما أن يكون من جانب الموضوع، أو من جانب المحمول، أو لا من جانب الموضوع ولا من جانب المحمول. [أ] أما الأول فهو الذي يكون الدوام فيه بحسب دوام ذات الموضوع كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان دائمًا، وهذه القضية هي الدائمة المطلقة فيكون المحمول موجودًا ما دام الموضوع موجودًا. [ب] وأما الثاني وهو الذي يكون الدوام فيه بحسب وصف الموضوع، فذلك الوصف إما أن يدوم بدوام ذات الموضوع؛ وحينئذ يدوم المحمول بدوام ذات الموضوع إذا كان دائمًا بدوام ذلك الوصف كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان ما دام إنسانًا وهذه القضية من قبيل الدائمة المطلقة لما أن المحمول موجود ما دام الموضوع موجودًا. وإما أن لا يدوم وحينئذ إما أن يكون مشروطًا بشرط الدوام لا غير كما في قولنا كل خمر مسكر ما دام خمرا وهذه هي العرفية العامة؛ وقد تسمى مطلقة منعكسة أيضًا لما أن السالبة منها تنعكس دون السالبة من المطلقة العامة. وإما أن لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا كما في قولنا كل خمر مسكر لا دائما بل ما دام خمرًا، وهذه هي العرفية الخاصة، فالعرفية العامة تعم العرفية الخاصة والمطلقة التي تقدم ذكرها.
As for permanence (al-dawam), it may be absolute (mutlaq) or it may not be, but rather be conditional (mashrut) upon a condition; that condition is either from the side of the subject (mawdu'), or from the side of the predicate (mahmul), or neither from the side of the subject nor the predicate. [A] As for the first, it is that in which permanence is according to the permanence of the essence of the subject, as in our statement 'Every human is an animal permanently' (da'iman), and this proposition is the absolute perpetual (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa), so the predicate exists as long as the subject exists. [B] As for the second, it is that in which permanence is according to a description (wasf) of the subject; that description either persists with the persistence of the essence of the subject; then the predicate persists with the persistence of the essence of the subject if it is permanent with the persistence of that description, as in our statement 'Every human is an animal as long as he is a human' (ma dama insanan), and this proposition is of the type of the absolute perpetual because the predicate exists as long as the subject exists. Or it does not persist; then either it is conditioned by the condition of permanence only, not otherwise, as in our statement 'Every wine is an intoxicant as long as it is wine' (ma dama khamran), and this is the generic 'urfiyya (al-'urfiyya al-'amma); it may also be called the absolute converse (mutlaqa mun'akisa) because its negative converts while the negative of the general absolute does not. Or it is not, but rather is conditioned by the condition of non-permanence (la dawam) as well, as in our statement 'Every wine is an intoxicant not permanently but as long as it is wine' (la da'iman bal ma dama khamran), and this is the specific 'urfiyya (al-'urfiyya al-khassa); so the generic 'urfiyya includes the specific 'urfiyya and the absolute (al-mutlaqa) which was mentioned earlier.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of propositions (qadaya) with respect to the modality of permanence (al-dawam). The author begins by stating that permanence can be either absolute (mutlaq), meaning unconditional perpetuity, or conditional (mashrut), i.e., restricted by a condition. This condition can derive from three sources: the subject, the predicate, or neither. The author then elaborates on the first two sources, both concerning the subject. The first case (marked [أ]) is when permanence is based on the very essence of the subject (dhat al-mawdu‘). The example given is 'Every human is an animal permanently,' meaning that as long as the human exists, the predicate 'animal' holds. This is called the 'absolute perpetual' (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa). The second case (marked [ب]) is when permanence is according to a description (wasf) of the subject. This description may itself be perpetual (i.e., coextensive with the subject's essence), in which case the proposition reverts to the absolute perpetual type. If the description is not perpetual, two subcases arise: either the permanence is conditioned only by the duration of the description (e.g., 'Every wine is an intoxicant as long as it is wine'), which yields the 'generic 'urfiyya' (al-'urfiyya al-'amma), also called 'absolute converse' because its negative undergoes conversion (insikās) unlike the general absolute; or it is conditioned both by the duration and by the non-permanence (i.e., it is true non-permanently but only for the duration), as in 'Every wine is an intoxicant not permanently but as long as it is wine,' yielding the 'specific 'urfiyya' (al-'urfiyya al-khassa). The generic 'urfiyya encompasses both the specific 'urfiyya and the earlier absolute perpetual. All examples are from Islamic logic: human, animal, wine, intoxicant — each relevant to fiqh and kalam. The student should understand that these distinctions are crucial for evaluating the truth conditions and logical properties of propositions in argumentation.

Key Terms
الدوام
الدائمة المطلقة
العرفية العامة
العرفية الخاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Generic 'urfiyya (al-'urfiyya al-'amma)
C) Specific 'urfiyya (al-'urfiyya al-khassa)
D) Absolute converse (al-mutlaqa al-mun'akisa)

Key Takeaway
Permanence propositions are classified based on whether the predicate holds absolutely or only under a condition of the subject's essence or description; understanding these types is essential for modal reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Permanence Based on Predicate Description: The 'Urfiyya Types
Author details predicate-based permanence conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify propositions with permanence conditioned by a predicate description into generic 'urfiyya (al-'urfiyya al-'amma) and specific 'urfiyya (al-'urfiyya al-khassa).

Core Idea
When a predicate's own description conditions its permanence, the proposition is 'urfiyya — with two degrees: generic and specific.

In this section, the author completes the classification of permanence by examining the predicate. When the permanence of a judgment depends on a description found in the predicate—like 'moving' in 'every animal is moving as long as it is moving'—we enter the territory of 'urfiyya propositions. This lesson will help you distinguish between the generic and specific forms of this type, and see how they relate to the absolute perpetual.

Guided Path
1
Locate the predicate description
Identify the wasf (description) attached to the predicate. For example, in 'Every animal is moving as long as it is moving', the description is 'moving' (mutaharrik).
The description is the condition for permanence.
2
Check if the description is perpetual or temporary
If the description persists with the subject's essence (e.g., 'human' and 'animal'), it reverts to the absolute perpetual. If not, proceed to the next step.
Perpetual descriptions are not distinct from the absolute perpetual.
3
Determine the condition of permanence
Is the description conditioned by permanence only (e.g., 'as long as it is moving') or also by non-permanence (e.g., 'not permanently but as long as it is moving')?
The presence of 'la da'iman' indicates non-permanence condition.
4
Classify as generic or specific
If conditioned by permanence only, it is generic 'urfiyya (al-'urfiyya al-'amma). If also by non-permanence, it is specific 'urfiyya (al-'urfiyya al-khassa).
The generic includes the specific and the absolute perpetual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ت] وأما الثالث وهو الذي يكون الدوام فيه بحسب وصف المحمول، فذلك الوصف إما أن يدوم بدوام ذات الموضوع؛ وحينئذ يدوم المحمول بدوام ذات الموضوع كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان ما دام حيوانا، وهذه القضية من قبيل الدائمة المطلقة أيضًا. وإما لا يدوم وحينئذ إما أن يكون مشروطًا بشرط الدوام لا غير كما في قولنا كل حيوان متحرك ما دام متحركا، وهذه هي العرفية العامة، من جانب المحمول. وإما أن لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا كما في قولنا كل حيوان متحرك لا دائما بل ما دام متحركا، وهذه هي العرفية الخاصة من جانب المحمول، فالعرفية العامة تعم العرفية الخاصة والدائمة التي تقدم ذكرها؛ وهذه الثلاثة أيضًا إنما تعتبر بحسب الدوام لا بحسب نفس الحكم. [ث] وأما الرابع وهو الذي لا يكون بحسب وصف الموضوع ولا يكون بحسب وصف المحمول كذلك. فالدوام فيه إما أن يكون بحسب وقت معين، وإنه قد يكون مشروطا بشرط ذلك الوقت لا غير كما في قولنا كل إنسان مدرك ما دام حيا، فإن كونه مدركًا دائم بدوام ذلك الوقت، وهذه هي الوقتية العامة؛ وقد لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام كما في قولنا كل إنسان مدرك لا دائما بل ما دام حيًّا وهذه هي الوقتية الخاصة. وأما أن يكون بحسب وقت غير معين، وإنه قد يكون مشروطًا بشرط ذلك الوقت كما في قولنا كل إنسان متحرك ما دام كاتبًا، فإن كونه متحركا دائم بدوام ذلك الوقت؛ وذلك الوقت غير معين وهذه هي المنتشرة العامة؛ وقد لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا كما في قولنا كل إنسان متحرك لا دائما بل ما دام كاتبا وهذه هي المنتشرة الخاصة: هذا إذا كان الدوام بحسب الوقت. فأما إذا لم يكن فذلك مثل قولنا كل متحرك غير ساكن دائمًا، وأما اللا دوام فإنه متعدد بحسب تعدد الدوام والمطلق منه مثل قولنا كل إنسان نائم لا دائمًا، وهذه الوجودية اللا دائمة، وإنها قد تكون ضرورية، وقد لا تكون لكنه لا يمكن أن تكون ضرورية مطلقة؛ وقد كان الدوام من لوازم الضرورة على ما عرف من قبل.
[C] As for the third, it is that in which permanence is according to a description of the predicate; that description either persists with the persistence of the essence of the subject; then the predicate persists with the persistence of the essence of the subject, as in our statement 'Every human is an animal as long as it is an animal' (ma dama hayawana), and this proposition is also of the type of the absolute perpetual. Or it does not persist; then either it is conditioned by the condition of permanence only, not otherwise, as in our statement 'Every animal is moving as long as it is moving' (ma dama mutaharrikan), and this is the generic 'urfiyya from the side of the predicate. Or it is not, but rather is conditioned by the condition of non-permanence as well, as in our statement 'Every animal is moving not permanently but as long as it is moving' (la da'iman bal ma dama mutaharrikan), and this is the specific 'urfiyya from the side of the predicate; so the generic 'urfiyya includes the specific 'urfiyya and the absolute perpetual which was mentioned earlier. And these three are also considered according to permanence, not according to the judgment itself. [D] As for the fourth, it is that which is not according to a description of the subject nor according to a description of the predicate. So permanence in it is either according to a specific time (waqt mu'ayyan); and it may be conditioned by the condition of that time only, not otherwise, as in our statement 'Every human is perceiving as long as he is alive' (ma dama hayyan), for his being perceiving is permanent with the permanence of that time, and this is the generic temporal (al-waqtiyya al-'amma); and it may not be, but rather is conditioned by the condition of non-permanence, as in our statement 'Every human is perceiving not permanently but as long as he is alive' (la da'iman bal ma dama hayyan), and this is the specific temporal (al-waqtiyya al-khassa). Or it is according to an unspecified time (ghayr mu'ayyan); and it may be conditioned by the condition of that time, as in our statement 'Every human is moving as long as he is writing' (ma dama katiban), for his being moving is permanent with the permanence of that time; and that time is unspecified, and this is the generic widespread (al-muntashira al-'amma); and it may not be, but rather is conditioned by the condition of non-permanence as well, as in our statement 'Every human is moving not permanently but as long as he is writing' (la da'iman bal ma dama katiban), and this is the specific widespread (al-muntashira al-khassa). This is if permanence is according to time. As for if it is not [according to time], then that is like our statement 'Every moving thing is not stationary permanently' (kull mutaharrik ghayr sakin da'iman). As for non-permanence (al-la dawam), it is multiple according to the multiplicity of permanence; the absolute (al-mutlaq) of it is like our statement 'Every human is sleeping not permanently' (la da'iman), and this is the existenceal non-permanent (al-wujudiyya la da'ima); it may be necessary (daruriyya) or not, but it cannot be absolutely necessary; and permanence (al-dawam) was among the concomitants (lawazim) of necessity (al-darura) as was known before.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of permanence in propositions. The author now covers the third and fourth sources of condition. The third type (marked [ت]) is when permanence depends on a description of the predicate. Similar to the second type, if the description itself is perpetual (coextensive with the subject), it reverts to the absolute perpetual. Otherwise, there are two subcases: the generic 'urfiyya (e.g., 'Every animal is moving as long as it is moving') and the specific 'urfiyya (e.g., 'Every animal is moving not permanently but as long as it is moving'). The note clarifies that these are considered according to permanence (bi-hasab al-dawam) not according to the judgment itself (nafs al-hukm), meaning the modality is on the duration, not on the content. The fourth type (marked [ث]) is when permanence is not based on either subject or predicate description but on time. This has two main divisions: specific time (waqt mu'ayyan) and unspecified time (ghayr mu'ayyan). For specific time, the example is 'Every human is perceiving as long as he is alive' (generic temporal) and its specific counterpart with non-permanence. For unspecified time, the example is 'Every human is moving as long as he is writing' (generic widespread) and its specific counterpart. The author then adds a remark about cases where permanence is not time-based, such as 'Every moving thing is not stationary permanently,' which is a necessary proposition? Finally, he discusses non-permanence (la dawam), noting that it can be absolute, as in 'Every human is sleeping not permanently,' which is called an existenceal non-permanent proposition; it may be necessary but cannot be absolutely necessary. He recalls that permanence is a concomitant (lazim) of necessity. This entire classification is critical for understanding the modal logic of propositions (jiha) in Islamic logic, used in evaluating syllogisms.

Key Terms
العرفية العامة من جانب المحمول
العرفية الخاصة من جانب المحمول
الدائمة المطلقة

D) Every human is mortal always.

Key Takeaway
The generic 'urfiyya from the predicate side is characterized by a predicate that holds only as long as a temporary description in the predicate itself persists.

Permanence Based on Time: Temporal and Widespread Propositions
Author completes classification with time-based permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between temporal (waqtiyya) and widespread (muntashira) propositions based on whether the time is specific or unspecified, and identify their generic and specific forms.

Core Idea
When permanence is time-based, the key factor is whether the time is specified (waqt mu'ayyan) or unspecified (ghayr mu'ayyan).

The author now turns to the fourth type of permanence: that which is not based on subject or predicate description but on time. This yields two main families: temporal propositions tied to a specific time (like life) and widespread propositions tied to an unspecified time (like writing). This lesson will help you parse these distinctions and recognize their generic and specific forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify if permanence is time-based
Check if the condition involves a time period (e.g., 'as long as he is alive', 'as long as he is writing'). If yes, it belongs to the fourth type.
Time-based conditions do not involve a description of subject or predicate.
2
Determine if the time is specific or unspecified
Specific time (waqt mu'ayyan): e.g., life is a defined period. Unspecified time (ghayr mu'ayyan): e.g., writing is an indefinite duration.
The distinction is between a fixed time span and an open-ended one.
3
Check for permanence or non-permanence condition
If the proposition only says 'as long as...' it is generic; if it adds 'not permanently but as long as...' it is specific.
This mirrors the 'urfiyya split.
4
Classify the proposition
For specific time: generic temporal (waqtiyya 'amma) or specific temporal (waqtiyya khassa). For unspecified time: generic widespread (muntashira 'amma) or specific widespread (muntashira khassa).
Example: 'Every human is perceiving as long as he is alive' is generic temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ت] وأما الثالث وهو الذي يكون الدوام فيه بحسب وصف المحمول، فذلك الوصف إما أن يدوم بدوام ذات الموضوع؛ وحينئذ يدوم المحمول بدوام ذات الموضوع كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان ما دام حيوانا، وهذه القضية من قبيل الدائمة المطلقة أيضًا. وإما لا يدوم وحينئذ إما أن يكون مشروطًا بشرط الدوام لا غير كما في قولنا كل حيوان متحرك ما دام متحركا، وهذه هي العرفية العامة، من جانب المحمول. وإما أن لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا كما في قولنا كل حيوان متحرك لا دائما بل ما دام متحركا، وهذه هي العرفية الخاصة من جانب المحمول، فالعرفية العامة تعم العرفية الخاصة والدائمة التي تقدم ذكرها؛ وهذه الثلاثة أيضًا إنما تعتبر بحسب الدوام لا بحسب نفس الحكم. [ث] وأما الرابع وهو الذي لا يكون بحسب وصف الموضوع ولا يكون بحسب وصف المحمول كذلك. فالدوام فيه إما أن يكون بحسب وقت معين، وإنه قد يكون مشروطا بشرط ذلك الوقت لا غير كما في قولنا كل إنسان مدرك ما دام حيا، فإن كونه مدركًا دائم بدوام ذلك الوقت، وهذه هي الوقتية العامة؛ وقد لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام كما في قولنا كل إنسان مدرك لا دائما بل ما دام حيًّا وهذه هي الوقتية الخاصة. وأما أن يكون بحسب وقت غير معين، وإنه قد يكون مشروطًا بشرط ذلك الوقت كما في قولنا كل إنسان متحرك ما دام كاتبًا، فإن كونه متحركا دائم بدوام ذلك الوقت؛ وذلك الوقت غير معين وهذه هي المنتشرة العامة؛ وقد لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا كما في قولنا كل إنسان متحرك لا دائما بل ما دام كاتبا وهذه هي المنتشرة الخاصة: هذا إذا كان الدوام بحسب الوقت. فأما إذا لم يكن فذلك مثل قولنا كل متحرك غير ساكن دائمًا، وأما اللا دوام فإنه متعدد بحسب تعدد الدوام والمطلق منه مثل قولنا كل إنسان نائم لا دائمًا، وهذه الوجودية اللا دائمة، وإنها قد تكون ضرورية، وقد لا تكون لكنه لا يمكن أن تكون ضرورية مطلقة؛ وقد كان الدوام من لوازم الضرورة على ما عرف من قبل.
[C] As for the third, it is that in which permanence is according to a description of the predicate; that description either persists with the persistence of the essence of the subject; then the predicate persists with the persistence of the essence of the subject, as in our statement 'Every human is an animal as long as it is an animal' (ma dama hayawana), and this proposition is also of the type of the absolute perpetual. Or it does not persist; then either it is conditioned by the condition of permanence only, not otherwise, as in our statement 'Every animal is moving as long as it is moving' (ma dama mutaharrikan), and this is the generic 'urfiyya from the side of the predicate. Or it is not, but rather is conditioned by the condition of non-permanence as well, as in our statement 'Every animal is moving not permanently but as long as it is moving' (la da'iman bal ma dama mutaharrikan), and this is the specific 'urfiyya from the side of the predicate; so the generic 'urfiyya includes the specific 'urfiyya and the absolute perpetual which was mentioned earlier. And these three are also considered according to permanence, not according to the judgment itself. [D] As for the fourth, it is that which is not according to a description of the subject nor according to a description of the predicate. So permanence in it is either according to a specific time (waqt mu'ayyan); and it may be conditioned by the condition of that time only, not otherwise, as in our statement 'Every human is perceiving as long as he is alive' (ma dama hayyan), for his being perceiving is permanent with the permanence of that time, and this is the generic temporal (al-waqtiyya al-'amma); and it may not be, but rather is conditioned by the condition of non-permanence, as in our statement 'Every human is perceiving not permanently but as long as he is alive' (la da'iman bal ma dama hayyan), and this is the specific temporal (al-waqtiyya al-khassa). Or it is according to an unspecified time (ghayr mu'ayyan); and it may be conditioned by the condition of that time, as in our statement 'Every human is moving as long as he is writing' (ma dama katiban), for his being moving is permanent with the permanence of that time; and that time is unspecified, and this is the generic widespread (al-muntashira al-'amma); and it may not be, but rather is conditioned by the condition of non-permanence as well, as in our statement 'Every human is moving not permanently but as long as he is writing' (la da'iman bal ma dama katiban), and this is the specific widespread (al-muntashira al-khassa). This is if permanence is according to time. As for if it is not [according to time], then that is like our statement 'Every moving thing is not stationary permanently' (kull mutaharrik ghayr sakin da'iman). As for non-permanence (al-la dawam), it is multiple according to the multiplicity of permanence; the absolute (al-mutlaq) of it is like our statement 'Every human is sleeping not permanently' (la da'iman), and this is the existenceal non-permanent (al-wujudiyya la da'ima); it may be necessary (daruriyya) or not, but it cannot be absolutely necessary; and permanence (al-dawam) was among the concomitants (lawazim) of necessity (al-darura) as was known before.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of permanence in propositions. The author now covers the third and fourth sources of condition. The third type (marked [ت]) is when permanence depends on a description of the predicate. Similar to the second type, if the description itself is perpetual (coextensive with the subject), it reverts to the absolute perpetual. Otherwise, there are two subcases: the generic 'urfiyya (e.g., 'Every animal is moving as long as it is moving') and the specific 'urfiyya (e.g., 'Every animal is moving not permanently but as long as it is moving'). The note clarifies that these are considered according to permanence (bi-hasab al-dawam) not according to the judgment itself (nafs al-hukm), meaning the modality is on the duration, not on the content. The fourth type (marked [ث]) is when permanence is not based on either subject or predicate description but on time. This has two main divisions: specific time (waqt mu'ayyan) and unspecified time (ghayr mu'ayyan). For specific time, the example is 'Every human is perceiving as long as he is alive' (generic temporal) and its specific counterpart with non-permanence. For unspecified time, the example is 'Every human is moving as long as he is writing' (generic widespread) and its specific counterpart. The author then adds a remark about cases where permanence is not time-based, such as 'Every moving thing is not stationary permanently,' which is a necessary proposition? Finally, he discusses non-permanence (la dawam), noting that it can be absolute, as in 'Every human is sleeping not permanently,' which is called an existenceal non-permanent proposition; it may be necessary but cannot be absolutely necessary. He recalls that permanence is a concomitant (lazim) of necessity. This entire classification is critical for understanding the modal logic of propositions (jiha) in Islamic logic, used in evaluating syllogisms.

Key Terms
الوقتية العامة
الوقتية الخاصة
المنتشرة العامة
المنتشرة الخاصة
الوجودية اللا دائمة

A) Every human is perceiving as long as he is alive.
B) Every human is moving as long as he is writing.
C) Every human is moving not permanently but as long as he is writing.
D) Every moving thing is not stationary permanently.

Key Takeaway
Time-based permanence splits into temporal (specific time) and widespread (unspecified time), each with generic and specific forms, and the existenceal non-permanent is a separate absolute non-permanence.

Types of Possibility: General, Special, Most Special
Author defines general and special possibility and distinguishes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general possibility (al-mumkin al-'amm), special possibility (al-mumkin al-khas), and most special possibility (al-mumkin al-akhas), and identify examples of each in modal propositions.

Core Idea
Possibility is defined by which necessity it lifts.

Modal logic goes beyond simple true/false. This passage introduces three levels of possibility: general, special, and most special. Each is defined by which side(s) of necessity are negated. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for analyzing modal arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where the modality of propositions about God, the world, and human actions carries weight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of possibility
Read the passage and find the three distinct terms: general possible (al-mumkin al-'amm), special possible (al-mumkin al-khas), and most special possible (al-mumkin al-akhas). Note that the passage also mentions another sense of 'possible' related to the future.
The text explicitly names these types; underline them.
2
Understand the criterion: lifting necessity
The key criterion is 'lifting necessity' (raf' al-darura). General possibility lifts necessity from one side (the contradictory side). Special possibility lifts necessity from both sides (affirmative and negative). Most special possibility excludes any necessity altogether.
Think of sides: the claim itself and its negation.
3
Locate the examples
The passage gives two examples: 'Every gold is meltable with special possibility' and 'Every human is a writer with most special possibility'. Note what each example illustrates about the modality.
These examples show the difference between lifting necessity from both sides vs. from any possible necessity.
4
Compare the relationships
The author states that special (khas) is more general than most special (akhas) but more special than general ('amm). Draw a hierarchy: general > special > most special.
This hierarchy is based on how many sides of necessity are negated.
5
Examine the additional meaning of 'possible'
The passage introduces a future-oriented possibility: something is possible if it is not necessary in all future times, regardless of present existence. This is not conditional on non-existence in the present.
This clarifies a common misunderstanding: present existence does not imply necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمتنع وهو الممكن الخاص. فالممكنة العامة هي التي حكم فيها بارتفاع الضرورة عن جانبها المخالف ؛ وإنها هي الأعم بالنسبة إلى ما عداها من القضايا كلا وجملة. والخاصة هي التي حكم فيها بارتفاع الضرورة عن جانبي الموافق والمخالف كما في قولنا كل ذهب ذائب بالإمكان الخاص وإنها هي الأعم بالنسبة إلى المشروطة الخاصة والعرفية الخاصة والوقتية والمنتشرة والوجودية اللا دائمة والممكنة بالإمكان الأخص أيضًا. فإنه يمكن أن يكون فيها الضرورة بوجه ما على خلاف الأخص فإنه لا يمكن فيها البتة كما في قولنا «كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الأخص»، فالخاص بالنسبة إلى العام هو الأخص، وبالنسبة إلى الأخص هو الأعم. وقد يقال ممكن ويفهم منه معنى آخر وهو أن لا يكون الالتفات في الاعتبار بحسب حال من أحوال الوجود من إيجاب أو سلب بل بحسب الاستقبال. فإذا لم يكن ضروريًا في كل وقت من الأوقات المفروضة في المستقبل فهو ممكن وإنه لا يكون مشروطًا بشرط أن لا يكون موجودًا في الحال كما هو عند البعض؛ إذ الوجود في الحال لا يخرجه إلى ضرورة الوجود وإلا لكان العدم في الحال يخرجه إلى ضرورة العدم كذلك؛ وذلك لا يخرجه بالاتفاق.
It is impossible and it is the special possible. The general possible proposition is that in which it is judged that necessity is lifted from its contradictory side; and it is the most general with respect to all other propositions, both particular and universal. The special possible is that in which it is judged that necessity is lifted from both the affirmative and negative sides, as in our saying 'Every gold is meltable with special possibility', and it is the most general with respect to the special conditional, the special customary, the temporal, the distributed, the non-permanent existential, and also the possible with the most special possibility. For it is possible that there is necessity in it in some manner, unlike the most special, for which there is no possibility at all, as in our saying 'Every human is a writer with the most special possibility'. So the special with respect to the general is the more special, and with respect to the more special it is the general. And sometimes 'possible' is said and another meaning is understood from it, namely that the consideration is not according to a state of existence, affirmation or negation, but according to the future. So if it is not necessary in every time of the supposed times in the future, then it is possible, and it is not conditioned by the condition that it not exist in the present, as is the case with some. For existence in the present does not remove it to the necessity of existence, otherwise non-existence in the present would remove it to the necessity of non-existence likewise. And that does not remove it by agreement.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes types of possibility (al-mumkin) in modal logic. It begins with 'special possibility' (al-mumkin al-khas), which is the impossible? Actually the text starts mid-sentence: 'يمتنع وهو الممكن الخاص' meaning 'It is impossible, and it is the special possible.' This likely refers back to a previous discussion. The author then defines general possible (al-mumkin al-'amm) as a proposition in which necessity is lifted from only one side (the contradictory). It is more general than other modal propositions. Special possible (al-mumkin al-khas) lifts necessity from both sides (affirmative and negative), as in 'Every gold is meltable with special possibility'—meaning it is possible that gold melts and possible that it does not, but not actual necessity. The special possible is more general than many specific modal types like the special conditional, etc. The author then distinguishes a more special (al-akhas) possibility, which is even narrower—e.g., 'Every human is a writer with the most special possibility'—where no necessity is involved at all. The relationships: special (al-khas) is more special than general (al-'amm), but more general than the most special (al-akhas). Finally, another meaning of 'possible' is introduced: considering future possibility without reference to present actuality. If something is not necessary in all future times, it is possible, and it is not conditioned on non-existence in the present. The author argues by consistency: present existence does not entail necessity of existence, just as present non-existence does not entail necessity of non-existence. This is a standard Kalam and logic argument about modalities.

الممكن الخاص
الضرورة

The Specificity of Malzum and Lazim and the Condition for Implication
Explains the relation of 'a'am and akhass between malzum and lazim and the condition for implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an implication holds by checking the realization of the necessary or the contradictory of the implicated, and distinguish between specificity in essence and actuality.

Core Idea
Implication requires actuality: either the lazim or the contradictory of the malzum must be realized.

One of the most subtle but powerful logical principles in Islamic logic is the relationship of specificity (akhass/a'am) between the malzum (implicated) and the lazim (necessary). This passage explains that the malzum is always more specific than the lazim, and that for implication to hold, it is not enough that they are related in essence—what matters is actual realization (tahaqquq). Mastering this distinction will protect you from false inferences in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the malzum and lazim
In a conditional statement like 'If A then B,' A is the malzum (implicated) and B is the lazim (necessary). The passage asserts that the malzum is always more specific (akhass) than the lazim.
Example: 'If it is wine, then it is forbidden.' Wine is malzum, forbidden is lazim.
2
Check the specificity relation
The malzum (A) is akhass (more specific) than the lazim (B) because every A is B but not every B is A. The passage also notes that the non-existence of B is more specific than the non-existence of A.
Counter-check: If wine is forbidden, is everything forbidden wine? No.
3
Apply the condition for implication
Implication (luzum) holds only if either the lazim or the contradictory of the malzum is realized. In other words, either B is true, or not-A is true. This is the key to distinguishing valid from invalid implications.
For 'If wine then forbidden,' if wine is present, forbidden must be true; if forbidden is false, wine must be false.
4
Distinguish essence from actuality
The lazim may be more specific than the malzum in essence (dhat) but not in actuality (tahaqquq). Implication depends on actuality, not essence. This prevents mistaken inferences where A implies B but B seems narrower.
Example: 'If it is a human, then it is rational.' In actuality, all humans are rational, so they are equal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالملزوم في كل صورة من هذه الصور أخص بالنسبة إلى اللازم ولا يكون هذا أخص من ذلك إلا وأن يكون عدم ذلك أخص من عدم هذا. والأخص هو المستلزم للغير من غير عكس، والأعم هو اللازم للغير من غير عكس. ثمَّ اللزوم لا يكون متحققا بين الأمرين إلا وأن يتحقق اللازم أو نقيض ملزومه؛ وذلك لأن اللازم / لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققا أو لا يكون. وإنما كان يتحقق أحدهما وهو إما اللازم أو نقيض الملزوم. وكذلك الملزوم لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققا أو لا يكون فإنه يلزم من لزوم أيهما كان أحد ما ذكرنا من الأمرين. ثم اللازم لازم لأحد الأمرين أعني اللازم والملزوم بالضرورة فإن أحدهما معيناً لا يخلو من أن يكون لازما أو ملزوما. وكذلك نقيض الملزوم فإنه لازم لأحد النقيضين أعني نقيض اللازم ونقيض الملزوم فإن أحدهما لا يخلو من أن يكون نقيض اللازم أو نقيض الملزوم. ولا يقال كيف هو واللازم من الأمرين أخص من أحدهما وهو إما اللازم أو الملزوم. وكذلك نقيض الملزوم فإنه أخص من أحد النقيضين وهو إما نقيض اللازم أو نقيض الملزوم ولا يمكن أن يكون اللازم أخص من ملزومه وقد كان من المحال أن يتحقق الملزوم دون اللازم، فإن اللازم من أحد الأمرين. وإن كان أخص بالنسبة إلى أحدهما بحسب الذات فلا يكون أخص منه في التحقق بل يكون مساويًا له، واللزوم بحسب التحقق. وكذلك نقيض الملزوم بالنسبة إلى أحد النقيضين فإنه وإن كان أخص من أحدهما بحسب الذات فقد
Thus the malzum (implicated) in every one of these forms is more specific (akhass) relative to the lazim (necessary), and it is not that this is more specific than that unless the non-existence of that is more specific than the non-existence of this. The more specific is that which implies the other without converse, and the more general (a'am) is that which is implied by the other without converse. Then implication is only realized between two things if either the necessary or the contradictory of the malzum is realized; that is because the necessary cannot but be either realized or not. Only one of them is realized, either the necessary or the contradictory of the malzum. Similarly, the malzum cannot but be either realized or not; for from the implication of either one of them, one of the two things mentioned follows. Then the necessary is necessary for one of the two things, i.e., the necessary and the malzum, necessarily; for one of them specifically cannot but be either a necessary or a malzum. Likewise, the contradictory of the malzum is necessary for one of the two contradictories, i.e., the contradictory of the necessary and the contradictory of the malzum; for one of them cannot but be either the contradictory of the necessary or the contradictory of the malzum. And it is not said that it is how it is while the necessary of the two things is more specific than one of them, i.e., either the necessary or the malzum. Likewise, the contradictory of the malzum is more specific than one of the two contradictories, i.e., either the contradictory of the necessary or the contradictory of the malzum, and it is impossible that the necessary be more specific than its malzum, yet it was impossible that the malzum be realized without the necessary, for the necessary is one of the two things. And if it is more specific relative to one of them in terms of essence, it is not more specific than it in terms of actuality; rather it is equal to it, and implication is in terms of actuality. Likewise, the contradictory of the malzum relative to one of the two contradictories, for even if it is more specific than one of them in terms of essence, yet...
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage explains the logical principle that in an incomplete implication, the malzum (implicated) is akhass and the lazim (necessary) is a'am. It then discusses the condition for implication: either the lazim or the contradictory of the malzum must be realized. This is a key logical principle in handling conditionals. The author also distinguishes between akhass by essence (dhat) and akhass in actuality (tahaqquq), noting that implication is based on actuality, so the lazim may be equal in actuality even if not in essence. This is a nuanced discussion that guards against misunderstandings about the nature of implication.

Key Takeaway

Contraposition of Conditional Propositions
Author explains that conditional propositions necessitate their contrapositives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of contraposition to conditional propositions in Islamic logical arguments.

Core Idea
Implication does not depend on existence; contraposition is valid for all conditionals.

In this passage, the author investigates the logical relationships between conditional propositions. He establishes that if one proposition necessarily implies another, then its contrapositive (the negation of the consequent implies the negation of the antecedent) is also necessarily true. This principle is crucial for constructing valid arguments in Islamic sciences like kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional form
Recognize that the passage deals with conditional propositions of the form 'If A then B' (shartiyya). The key terms are luzum (implication) and naqid (contradictory).
The author uses general terms 'thing' (shay') to stand for propositions.
2
Extract the main claim about implication
The author asserts that a conditional proposition necessarily implies other conditionals that agree or disagree in implication and opposition. Focus on the specific claim: If A implies B, then not-B implies not-A.
This is the law of contraposition.
3
Understand the argument against existence-dependence
The author argues that implication is a logical relation independent of actual existence. If it depended on existence, then contraposition would not hold, which is impossible.
He uses a reductio: if A implies B, it cannot be that not-B does not imply not-A while both pairs exist.
4
Apply contraposition to an Islamic example
Take a conditional from kalam: 'If the world is temporally originated, then it has a creator.' Its contrapositive is 'If the world has no creator, then it is not temporally originated.' Check that this preserves truth.
This example is directly from Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في أن القضية الشرطية فإنها مستلزمة لغيرها من الشرطيات موافقة في اللزوم والعناد ومخالفة فيهما ]
أما القضية الشرطية فإنها مستلزمة لغيرها من الشرطيات موافقة في اللزوم والعناد، ومخالفة فيهما. وكونها مستلزمة لا تفتقر في الوجود إلى وجودها. ولا وجود ما يلازمها كذلك وإلا لا يلزم من كون الشيء مستلزما للشيء أن يكون نقيض ذلك الشيء مستلزما لنقيضه. فإن من المحال أن يكون هذا الشيء مستلزما لذلك الشيء، ونقيض ذلك الشيء مستلزما لنقيضه مع تحقق كل واحد من الشيئين وتحقق كل واحد من النقيضين. وكما أن كونها مستلزمة لا تفتقر إلى هذا وذلك فكذلك كونها معاندة. ومن زعم بأن نقيض الشيء إذا كان ممتنعا لا يلزم من ذلك النقيض نقيض الملزوم فزعمه باطل لما مرّ
Section: [On that the conditional proposition is necessarily implying other conditionals in agreement in implication and opposition, and disagreement in both.] As for the conditional proposition, it necessarily implies other conditionals in agreement in implication and opposition, and disagreement in both. And its being necessarily implying does not depend in existence on its existence, nor on the existence of what entails it similarly; otherwise it would not follow from a thing's being a necessary condition for a thing that the contradictory of that thing is a necessary condition for its contradictory. For it is impossible that this thing is a necessary condition for that thing, and the contradictory of that thing is a necessary condition for its contradictory, while each of the two things and each of the two contradictories are realized. And just as its being necessarily implying does not depend on this and that, so too its being contradictory. And whoever claims that if the contradictory of a thing is impossible, then that contradictory does not necessitate the contradictory of the necessary condition, his claim is false for what has passed.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a logical analysis of conditional propositions (qadiyya shartiyya), focusing on the relationships of implication (luzum) and opposition ('inad). The author states that a conditional proposition necessarily implies other conditionals that agree with it in implication and opposition, or disagree in both. He then clarifies that the implication relation does not depend on the actual existence of the propositions; rather, it is a logical relationship. If it did depend on existence, then from the fact that A implies B, it would not follow that not-B implies not-A, which is false. In fact, it is impossible for A to imply B and for not-B to imply not-A if both A&B and not-A&not-B are simultaneously true? The author argues that implication (luzum) between two propositions is such that the contrapositive (naqid al-lazim implies naqid al-malzum) holds necessarily. He then equates this with 'inad (opposition): if A implies B, then A is opposed to not-B? Actually, 'inad here likely refers to logical opposition between conditionals. He refutes a claim that if the contradictory of a thing is impossible, then that contradictory does not imply the contradictory of the necessary condition. This is a subtle point about the logic of conditionals, affirming the validity of contraposition. The passage is dense but illustrates the author's engagement with the logical properties of conditional statements.

A conditional proposition necessarily implies its contrapositive, independent of the actual truth of its components; this is a foundational rule for valid inference in Islamic logic.

Necessary Consequences in Universal Propositions
Author analyzes necessary logical relationships between universal affirmatives and negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify necessary and non-necessary consequences of universal affirmative and negative propositions, and evaluate dialectical objections about necessity relationships.

Core Idea
Necessary consequences are not reciprocal: the original implies them, but they do not imply the original.

This dense passage unpacks the intricate web of necessary relationships between universal affirmatives and negatives. We will explore how truth flows from the original proposition to its necessary consequences, and crucially, where it stops. A dialectical objection and its refutation teach us about the logic of generality and specificity in necessity claims.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish necessary from non-necessary consequences
Identify the criterion: a necessary consequence must be true whenever the original is true; if it can be false, it is not necessary. The passage defines al-lawazim and al-la mustalzima.
Non-necessary consequences can exist without the original.
2
Map the affirmative-negative relationship
Observe that from a universal affirmative, both negative and affirmative consequences follow. The passage states affirmatives among consequences include the negatives, and vice versa.
This does not mean each consequence individually is both affirmative and negative; the set of consequences includes both types.
3
Analyze the universal negative case
For a universal negative, the same pattern holds: its necessary consequences include both affirmatives and negatives. The necessity is direct, not mediated through other consequences.
The author emphasizes that consequences are necessary 'in themselves' for the original negative.
4
Examine the dialectical objection
An objector claims: if something is necessarily necessary for one of two opposites, it must be necessary for that specific one if not for the other. Identify the misstep: confusing generality with specificity.
The objector's statement is at the end of the passage.
5
Apply the refutation
The author refutes by stating that 'one of them not specifically' is more general than 'this specifically', and generality does not imply specificity. This preserves the asymmetry of necessity.
The refutation uses the principle that from a general necessity, specific necessity does not follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للسالبة فيكون الموجبات في اللوازم متضمنة للسوالب، والسوالب للموجبات كذلك. والصدق في الكل من اللوازم فإن انتفاء الصدق فيها مما ينافي الصدق في الأصل . وأما الكلية من السوالب فكما أن الموجبات اللازمة لتلك الموجبة التي هي الأصل من لوازم هذه السالبة فكذلك السوالب اللازمة لتلك الموجبة؛ إذ الجملة لازمة هذه السالبة أيضًا لا من حيث إنها لازمة للموجبات التي هي لازمة وإن كانت هي من لوازم تلك الموجبات بل من حيث إنها لازمة بنفسها؛ فإن اللوازم على الإطلاق هي اللوازم بهذا الاعتبار لا بذلك. وعلى هذا في الجزئيات اللازمة لتلك الموجبة موجبة وسالبة؛ إذ الجملة لازمة لهذه السالبة أيضًا وإلا لكذبت السالبة التي هي الأصل فيلزم من صدقها إذن صدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان (ج) د » فـ «أب»، وقد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د». وكذلك ليس كلما كان «أب» فليس (ج) دا ، وليس كلما كان «ج د» فليس «أب»، وعلى هذا بالنسبة إلى جميع اللوازم التي مرَّ ذكرها. وأما اللا مستلزمة فهي يمكن وجودها بدون الموجبة الكلية، ويمكن وجود الموجبة الكلية بدونها كذلك كما في قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان (أ ب) فـ ( د)، وإنها من حيث هي هي لا تكون مستلزمة للموجبات التي مرَّ ذكرها ؛ ولا للسالبات كذلك. أما للموجبات فلأنه يمكن أن لا يكون الشيء مستلزما للشيء بعينه، ولا لنقيض ذلك الشيء كذلك وإن كان مستلزما لأحد النقيضين مطلقًا. ولو كان كذلك فلا يلزم من نفي اللزوم في أحد الطرفين إثبات اللزوم، وفي الطرف الآخر فلا تكون الموجبة لازمة إذن.
ثم لقائل أن يقول إذا كان الشيء مستلزما لأحدهما بالضرورة كان مستلزما لهذا بعينه إذا لم يكن مستلزما لذلك، وبالعكس وإلا لا يكون مستلزما لا لهذا ولا لذلك كذلك، والكلام في كونه مستلزما إما لهذا وإما لذلك. لكنا نقول:
أحدهما لا بعينه أعم من هذا بعينه، ومن ذلك بعينه أيضًا؛ ولا يلزم من كون الأعم لازما للشيء أن يكون الأخص منه لازما لذلك الشيء. وأما للسوالب فلأنه يمكن أن لا يكون الشيء مستلزما للشيء ويكون مستلزما لنقيضه كما مر من قبل. ولو كان كذلك فلا يلزم من نفي اللزوم في أحد الطرفين نفي اللزوم في الطرف الآخر فلا تكون السالبة لازمة إذن وكيف يكون والشيء لا يكون لازما للشيء إلا ولا يمكن أن لا يكون عند تحقيق ذلك الشيء، فلو كان محتملا أن يكون وأن لا يكون فلا مجال لكونه لازما البتة؛ وهذا ظاهر.
For the negative, so the affirmatives among necessary consequences include the negatives, and the negatives include the affirmatives likewise. Truth in all necessary consequences is such that the absence of truth in them contradicts truth in the original. As for the universal negative, just as the affirmatives necessary for that affirmative which is the original are among the consequences of this negative, so too the negatives necessary for that affirmative; for the totality is necessary for this negative as well, not insofar as it is necessary for the affirmatives that are necessary, even if they are among the consequences of those affirmatives, but insofar as it is necessary in itself. For necessary consequences in an absolute sense are consequences in this respect, not that. And on this basis, the particular propositions necessary for that affirmative are both affirmative and negative; for the totality is necessary for this negative as well, otherwise the original negative would be false. From its truth, then, follows the truth of our statement: 'It may be that when (J) is D, then A is B', and 'It may be that when A is B, then J is D'. Likewise, not whenever A is B is it false that J is D, and not whenever J is D is it false that A is B. And this applies to all the necessary consequences that have been mentioned. As for the non-necessary, it can exist without the universal affirmative, and the universal affirmative can exist without it, as in our statement: 'Absolutely never when A is B is it that D', and it in itself is not necessary for the affirmatives that have been mentioned, nor for the negatives. As for the affirmatives, because it is possible for a thing not to be necessary for that very thing, nor for the opposite of that thing, even if it is necessary for one of the two opposites absolutely. And if it were so, then from the negation of necessity in one of the two sides, the affirmation of necessity does not follow, and in the other side, then the affirmative is not necessary. Then one might say: if the thing is necessarily necessary for one of them, it is necessary for this very one if it is not necessary for that one, and conversely; otherwise it is not necessary for this one nor for that one, and the discussion is about its being necessary either for this or for that. But we say: 'One of them not specifically' is more general than 'this specifically' and than 'that specifically'; and from something more general being a necessary consequence of a thing, it does not follow that the more specific is a necessary consequence of that thing. As for the negatives, because it is possible for a thing not to be necessary for a thing and to be necessary for its opposite, as previously. And if it were so, then from the negation of necessity in one of the two sides, the negation of necessity in the other side does not follow; thus the negative is not necessary. How could it be, when a thing is only a necessary consequence of a thing if it cannot not be when that thing is realized. So if it is possible that it be and that it not be, there is no room for its being a necessary consequence at all. This is clear.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage is a logical analysis of the relationship between universal affirmative propositions and their necessary consequences (al-lawazim). The author begins by stating that in the realm of necessary consequences, affirmative propositions contain within themselves the negatives, and negatives likewise contain the affirmatives. This means that if a universal affirmative is true, certain negative propositions must also be true as its necessary consequences, and vice versa. The author emphasizes that truth in all necessary consequences is such that if any of them were false, the original proposition would also be false; thus they are inseparable.

The discussion then moves to universal negatives. The author explains that the same relationship holds: the necessary consequences of the original affirmative (which is the original universal) include both affirmatives and negatives that are necessary for the universal negative as well. However, the necessity is not because these consequences are necessary for the affirmative that is a consequence, but because they are necessary in themselves for the original negative.

The author then addresses particular propositions (al-juz'iyyat) that are necessary consequences of the original affirmative. He notes that these too come in both affirmative and negative forms, and they must be true for the original negative to be true. He provides examples using variables: 'It may be that when J is D, then A is B' and 'It may be that when A is B, then J is D', and also discusses negations of these conditionals.

Next, the concept of non-necessary (al-la mustalzima) is introduced. These are propositions that can exist without the original universal affirmative, and the original can exist without them. An example is given: 'Absolutely never when A is B is it that D.' Such a proposition is not a necessary consequence of the original affirmatives or negatives. The author explains why: for affirmatives, it is possible that a thing is not necessary for a specific thing nor for its opposite, even if it is necessary for one of the two opposites absolutely. This means that the absence of necessity in one case does not necessitate necessity in the other.

A dialectical objection is then presented: someone might argue that if a thing is necessarily necessary for one of two opposites, then it must be necessary for that specific one if it is not necessary for the other. The author refutes this by stating that 'one of them not specifically' is more general than 'this specifically', and generality does not entail specificity. For negatives, he reiterates that it is possible for a thing to be necessary for the opposite of something without being necessary for that thing itself. Therefore, from the negation of necessity in one side, the negation of necessity in the other side does not follow. The passage concludes that if a proposition could possibly be true or false without affecting the original, then it cannot be a necessary consequence.

Key Terms
اللوازم (al-lawazim): Propositions that must be true whenever the original proposition is true, but the original may not be true when they are true. [Note: This usage is standard in logical works; in later logical texts, 'lazim' also appears in discussions of conditional proof.]
الموجبة الكلية (al-mujiba al-kulliya): A universal affirmative proposition, e.g., 'Every A is B'. [Note: No significant shift.]
السالبة الكلية (al-saliba al-kulliya): A universal negative proposition, e.g., 'No A is B'. [Note: No significant shift.]
اللا مستلزمة (al-la mustalzima): A proposition that is not a necessary consequence; it can be true or false independently of the original. [Note: This term is less common in later logic, where 'lazim' and 'ghayr lazim' are used instead.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The affirmative implies only negative consequences.
C) The affirmative implies both affirmative and negative consequences, but the consequences do not necessarily imply the affirmative.
D) The affirmative implies only affirmative consequences.

Key Takeaway
Necessary consequences of a universal proposition include both affirmatives and negatives, but the relation is one-way: the original implies its necessary consequences, not the reverse.

Mutual Implication and Conversion of Particular Conditionals
Author lists particular conditional forms and states their mutual implication and conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of mutual implication and conversion to particular conditional propositions in Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Affirmative particular conditionals mutually imply and convert; the same holds for negatives.

In this passage, the author enumerates four types of quantified conditional propositions and states that the affirmatives among them are mutually implying and converting, and similarly for the negatives. This means that from one true particular conditional, you can infer others. Understanding this rule helps in evaluating chains of conditional arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four quantified conditionals
The passage lists: particular affirmative ('قد يكون إذا كان أب فـ جد'), particular negative ('قد يكون إذا كان أب فليس جد'), universal affirmative ('كلما كان أب فـ جد'), universal negative ('كلما كان أب فليس جد'). Note the key quantifiers 'قد يكون' (it may be) and 'كلما' (whenever).
These are conditional analogs to categorical A, E, I, O.
2
Understand mutual implication (mutalāzima)
For affirmatives: if any one is true, all affirmatives are true. For negatives: if any one is true, all negatives are true. For example, if the particular affirmative 'قد يكون إذا كان أب فـ جد' is true, then the universal affirmative 'كلما كان أب فـ جد' must also be true, and vice versa.
This holds only within the same quality (affirmative/negative), not across.
3
Apply conversion (muta'ākisa)
Each proposition converts: swap antecedent and consequent while preserving quantifier and quality. From 'قد يكون إذا كان أب فـ جد' we infer 'قد يكون إذا كان جد فـ أب'. Conversion is valid for all four types because of mutual implication.
Conversion here is for conditionals, not categorical conversion.
4
Practice with a fiqh example
Take the particular affirmative 'قد يكون إذا كان هذا الشراب نبيذًا فهو مسكر' (It may be that if this drink is nabidh, it is intoxicating). By mutual implication, the universal 'كلما كان هذا الشراب نبيذًا فهو مسكر' is implied. By conversion, 'قد يكون إذا كان هذا الشراب مسكرًا فهو نبيذ' is implied. Check that this follows the rule.
The truth of the original ensures the others are logically true, regardless of factual accuracy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د». قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فليس «ج د». كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د». كلما كان «أب» فليس «ج د». فالموجبات من هذه الجزئيات متلازمة ومتعاكسة والسالبات كذلك.
It may be that if 'A' is 'B' then 'C' is 'D'. It may be that if 'A' is 'B' then 'C' is not 'D'. Whenever 'A' is 'B' then 'C' is 'D'. Whenever 'A' is 'B' then 'C' is not 'D'. So the affirmatives among these particulars are mutually implying and converting, and the negatives likewise.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four types of conditional propositions with particular quantification: the particular affirmative, particular negative, universal affirmative, and universal negative. It then states that the affirmative ones are mutually implying (mutalāzima) and converting (muta'ākisa), and the same holds for the negatives. This means that if one affirmative particular conditional is true, the others are true as well, and their converses also hold. For example, from 'it may be that if A is B then C is D' we infer 'it may be that if C is D then A is B', and similarly for the universal forms. The author emphasizes that this mutual implication and conversion apply specifically to these quantified conditional propositions.

Key Terms

متعاكسة
الجزئيات

Checkpoint

A) 'قد يكون إذا كان هذا الشيء مسكرًا فهو خمر' (It may be that if this thing is intoxicating, it is wine)

D) 'كلما كان هذا الشيء مسكرًا فهو خمر' (Whenever this thing is intoxicating, it is wine)

Key Takeaway
Affirmative (and separately negative) particular conditionals are mutually implying and convert; knowing one gives you all affirmatives (or all negatives) and their converses.

Implications of an Exclusive Disjunction
Explains logical implications from a true exclusive disjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive valid conditional implications from a true exclusive disjunction (man'ia min al-khuluww) and identify fallacious inferences.

Core Idea
In an exclusive disjunction, only negating one disjunct implies the other; affirming one yields no conclusion.

This passage from the logic text delves into the logical implications that follow when an exclusive disjunction (man'ia min al-khuluww) is true. It uses the example 'Every D is either H or not Z' to illustrate which conditional statements are valid and which are not. Understanding these implications is essential for drawing correct inferences in any discipline that uses disjunctive reasoning, especially in Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh) and theology (kalam).

Guided Path
1
Identify the exclusive disjunction
Locate the statement 'Every D is either H or not Z'. Recognize that this is a man'ia min al-khuluww, where both disjuncts cannot be false but can be true.
The disjunction is exclusive in the sense of non-emptiness, not mutual exclusivity.
2
Derive valid implications from negation
From the truth of the disjunction, if one disjunct is false, the other must be true. Thus, 'If it is not H, then it is not Z' is valid. Similarly, 'If it is not not Z (i.e., if it is Z), then it is H' is valid.
These are the only direct one-way implications from the disjunction.
3
Recognize invalid implications from affirmation
From the truth of a disjunct, no conclusion follows about the other. For example, 'If it is H, then it is Z' is invalid, as is 'If it is not Z, then it is H'. Both disjuncts could be true, so neither force the other.
This is a common point of confusion.
4
Understand the self-evident nature
The author notes that these implications are known by reflection (ta'ammul) and need no formal proof. A lack of such implication would indicate that the original proposition was not a true exclusive disjunction.
This highlights the foundational role of disjunctions in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك في المانعة من الخلو فإنه إذا صدق قولنا كل «د» إما «هـ» وإما ليس «ز»، صدق قولنا إنه إذا لم يكن (هـ) فهو ليس ز ) ، وإذا لم يكن ليس ز فهو (هـ). وكما أن عدم كونه (هـ) مستلزم لوجود ذلك وهو ليس «ز»، فكذلك لوجود ما يكون من لوازمه؛ وإنه متعدد حسب تعدد ما يكون من اللوازم وكذلك عدم كونه ليس ((د)) لوجود (هـ)؛ ولوجود ما يكون من لوازمه وإنه متعدد حسب تعدد ما يكون من اللوازم أيضًا، ولا يلزم من كونه (هـ) لا كونه ز» ولا ليس كونه ز». وكذلك لا يلزم من كونه ليس «ز» لا كونه «ه» ولا ليس كونه «ه» كذلك. ثم اللزوم في جميع هذه المتّصلات من جملة ما لا حاجة له إلى البيان بل يعرف بالتأمل، وإن عدم اللزوم مما يستلزم عدم العناد في الأصل، وهذا ظاهر.
Similarly, in the exclusive disjunction (man'ia min al-khuluww): if our statement 'Every D is either H or not Z' is true, then our statement 'If it is not H, then it is not Z' is true, and 'If it is not not Z, then it is H' is true. And just as its not being H entails the existence of that which is not Z, likewise it entails the existence of what is a consequence of it; and it is multiple according to the multiplicity of consequences. And likewise, its not being not-d implies the existence of H; and the existence of what is a consequence of it is multiple according to the multiplicity of consequences. And it does not follow from its being H that it is Z or that it is not Z. Similarly, it does not follow from its being not Z that it is H or that it is not H. Then the implication in all these conditionals is among things that need no proof; rather it is known by contemplation, and the lack of implication necessitates the lack of contrariety (ʿinād) in the original. This is clear.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage explains the logical implications that follow from a true exclusive disjunction (man'ia min al-khuluww). The author gives an example: 'Every D is either H or not Z.' From this, we can derive two conditional statements: (1) If it is not H, then it is not Z; (2) If it is not not Z, then it is H. These follow directly. He then elaborates on the multiplicity of consequences depending on what is considered a 'consequence' (lāzim). He clarifies that from 'D is H' alone we cannot infer 'D is Z' or 'D is not Z', and similarly from 'D is not Z' we cannot infer 'D is H' or 'D is not H'. The author asserts that these implications are self-evident through reflection (ta'ammul), and that a lack of such implication would entail a lack of contrariety (ʿinād) in the original proposition. This is a foundational point for understanding how exclusive disjunctions govern logical relations.

Key Terms
المانعة من الخلو
اللزوم
المتصلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the exclusive disjunction (man'ia min al-khuluww) 'Every D is either H or not Z', which of the following implications is valid?
A) If D is H, then D is not Z.
B) If D is not H, then D is not Z.
C) If D is not Z, then D is not H.
D) If D is Z, then D is not H.

Key Takeaway
In an exclusive disjunction, only the negation of a disjunct implies the other; affirmation of a disjunct yields no necessary conclusion about the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Write down the two valid conditional implications and explain why the implication 'If the Quran is created, then it is not uncreated' is not valid according to the passage.

Task 2: Consider the exclusive disjunction 'Every human action is either obligatory (wajib) or forbidden (haram).' Identify which of the following implications are valid according to the lesson: (a) If an action is not obligatory, then it is forbidden; (b) If an action is forbidden, then it is not obligatory; (c) If an action is obligatory, then it is forbidden.
