Affirmative Consequences from an Inclusive Universal Disjunction
Lists affirmative consequences from universal inclusive disjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive all affirmative disjunctions that necessarily follow from a universal affirmative inclusive disjunction (e.g., 'Every A is either B or C').

Core Idea
A single true disjunction generates infinitely many new true disjunctions via negation, equality, and contrariety.

In classical Islamic logic, understanding the implications (lawāzim) of a proposition is essential for constructing valid syllogisms. This lesson focuses on deriving affirmative disjunctions from a universal affirmative inclusive disjunction. The author demonstrates that from a statement like 'Every A is either B or C', we can systematically construct other disjunctions that must also be true, using relationships such as negation, equality, and contrariety between the terms. This expands our logical toolkit and prepares us for complex argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original disjunction
Start with the given universal affirmative inclusive disjunction: 'Always every A is either B or C'. Note that this is a māniʿa al-jamʿ wa al-khuluww (true exclusive disjunction where both cannot be true and both cannot be false).
2
Construct by replacing a disjunct with its negation
Replace either B or C with its negation. For example, 'Always every A is either B or not B' must be true because if 'Every A is either B or C' is true, then every A is either B or not B (since if A is not B, it is C, and C implies not B).
The negation of a disjunct yields a tautological disjunction.
3
Construct by replacing a disjunct with something equal to it
If something is equal to B (or C), then the disjunction with that equal also holds. For instance, 'Always every A is either B or what is equal to C' is true.
Equality here means logical equivalence or definitional equality.
4
Construct by replacing a disjunct with something contrary to it
If something is contrary (opposite) to B, then 'Always every A is either B or what is contrary to B' is true. The author calls this 'what is in opposition (al-munāfī)'.
Contrariety (ʿinād) ensures the derived disjunction remains true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني من المباحث فذلك في اللوازم من المنفصلات للموجبة الكلية التي مرَّ ذكرها وهي مانعة الجمع والخلو وللسالبة الكلية كذلك. أما الموجبة فنقول: إذا صدق قولنا دائما كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج»؛ صدق من المنفصلات الحقيقة ما يتركب من عين المقدم ونقيض الباء فيقال دائما كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما ليس «ب». وكذلك من عين المقدم وعين ما يساوي النقيض، ومن عين المقدم وعين ما يساوي الجيم، وإنه متعدد تعدد المساوي لا محالة، ومن المساوي لهذا. ولذلك كذلك يتركب من عين المقدم وعين ما ينافي الباء وإنه متعدد تعدد المنافي أيضًا، ومن عين المقدم وعين الباء، وإنه متعدد غاية التعداد. وكذلك يتركب من مقتضى الباء والجيم، ومن المساوي لهذا النقيض. ولذلك كذلك، فهذه هي الموجبات من اللوازم. وأما السوالب فإنها متعددة حسب تعدد هذه الموجبات. فإن العناد لا يكون بين الشيء وغيره إلا وأن يكون اللزوم متحققا بينه وبين نقيض ذلك الشيء لما مر واللزوم من جملة ما يستلزم صدق السالبة فيقال إذن ليس البتة «أ» إما «ب» وإما ليس «ج»، وليس البتة إما «ب» وإما المساوي للنقيض أعني نقيض الجيم. وعلى هذا في الغير: فهذه هي اللوازم للموجبة الكلية. وأما للسالبة الكلية فالسالبة الكلية قد تكون مستلزمة للموجبة الكلية وقد لا تكون أما المستلزمة فإنها كما هي مستلزمة للموجبة فكذلك لجميع ما يكون. من اللوازم للموجبة من المنفصلات الحقيقية وغير الحقيقية موجبة وسالبة. وأما اللا مستلزمة فإنها لا تكون مستلزمة لا للموجبات ولا للسوالب لما مرّ من قبل في المنفصلات. ثمَّ المستلزمة منهما تعرف من بعد بأنها ما هي، وكيف هي، وكذلك اللا مستلزمة هذا هو الكلام في المانعة من الجمع والخلو. وأما في المانعة من الجمع فنقول : إذا صدق قولنا دائما كل «ج» إما «ب» وإما د»، صدق قولنا كل (ج) إما «ب» وإما «خ» غير «ب». وكذلك كل «ج» إما «د» وإما غير «د»، والانفصال فيهما انفصال حقيقي. وقد صدق أيضًا كل "ج " إما (ب) و إما (هـ)؛ و (هـ) أخص من «د»، وكذلك كل «ج» إما «د» وإما «ز»، و «ز» أخص من «ب». والانفصال فيهما نحو الانفصال في الأصل فهذه هي اللوازم من الموجبات. وأما للسوالب فمثل قولنا ليس البتة «ج» إما أن تكون «ب» وإما أن لا يكون «د»، وليس البتة إما أن تكون «د» وإما أن لا تكون «ب». وعلى هذا في المانعة من الخلو ، فإنه إذا صدق قولنا كل «د» إما «ه» وإما لیس از صدق قولنا كل (د) إما (ز) وإما ليس (هـ). وكذلك إما «ه» وإما ليس «ب»، وليس (ب) أعم من ليس «ز»، والانفصال فيه نحو الانفصال في الأصل. وأما ما يكون من السوالب فمثل قولنا ليس البتة «د» إما «ه» وإما «ز»، وكذلك ليس البتة إما ليس (هـ) وإما ليس «ز».
As for the second inquiry: it is about the consequences (lawāzim) from the disjunctions for the universal affirmative, which have been mentioned previously, that is the inclusive and exclusive disjunction (māniʿa al-jamʿ wa al-khuluww), and for the universal negative likewise. As for the affirmative, we say: if our statement 'Always every A is either B or C' is true, then among the true disjunctions (al-munfaṣilāt al-ḥaqīqiyya) is what is composed of the very antecedent and the negation of B, so it is said 'Always every A is either B or not B'. Likewise, from the very antecedent and what is equal to the negation, and from the very antecedent and what is equal to C, and it is multiple necessarily according to the multiplicity of what is equal. And from what is equal to this. And likewise, it is composed from the very antecedent and what is contrary to B and it is multiple according to the multiplicity of contraries, and from the very antecedent and B itself, and it is multiple to the utmost. Likewise, it is composed from the requirement (muqtaḍā) of B and C, and from what is equal to this negation. And likewise. So these are the affirmative consequences. As for the negative ones, they are multiple according to the multiplicity of these affirmatives. For contrariety (ʿinād) does not exist between a thing and another unless implication is realized between it and the negation of that other, as has passed. And implication is among that which entails the truth of the negative, so it is said: 'Not at all is A either B or not C', and 'Not at all is A either B or the equal of the negation, i.e., the negation of C'. And so on for others. So these are the consequences for the universal affirmative. As for the universal negative, the universal negative may entail the universal affirmative or may not. As for what entails, just as it entails the affirmative, so it entails all that are among the consequences of the affirmative from the real and non-real disjunctions, affirmative and negative. As for what does not entail, it does not entail either affirmatives or negatives, as has preceded regarding disjunctions. Then what entails from these two is known later by what it is and how it is, and likewise what does not entail. This is the discussion regarding the inclusive and exclusive disjunction (māniʿa al-jamʿ wa al-khuluww). As for the exclusive disjunction (māniʿa al-jamʿ), we say: if our statement 'Always every J is either B or D' is true, then our statement 'Every J is either B or K other than B' is true. Likewise, 'Every J is either D or other than D', and the disjunction in them is a real disjunction. And also true is 'Every J is either B or H', where H is more specific (akhaṣṣ) than D, and likewise 'Every J is either D or Z', where Z is more specific than B. And the disjunction in them is similar to the disjunction in the original. So these are the consequences from affirmatives. As for the negatives, for example our statement: 'Not at all is J either B or not D', and 'Not at all is J either D or not B'. And similarly for the exclusive disjunction (māniʿa min al-khuluww): if our statement 'Every D is either H or not Z' is true, then our statement 'Every D is either Z or not H' is true. Likewise, 'Every D is either H or not B', where 'not B' is more general (aʿamm) than 'not Z', and the disjunction in it is similar to the disjunction in the original. As for what is from negatives, it is like our statement: 'Not at all is D either H or Z', and likewise 'Not at all is D either not H or not Z'.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a systematic exposition of the logical consequences (lawāzim) that follow from universal affirmative and negative disjunctions, both inclusive (māniʿa al-jamʿ wa al-khuluww) and exclusive (māniʿa al-jamʿ and māniʿa min al-khuluww). The author first addresses the universal affirmative disjunction 'Every A is either B or C' (which is inclusive). From this, many other disjunctions necessarily follow, such as 'Every A is either B or not B', 'Every A is either B or what is equal to the negation of B', and so on. The author explains that these disjunctions are constructed from the antecedent and various related terms (like the negation, the equal, the contrary, etc.). The number of consequences depends on the multiplicity of these relations. Then, the author turns to the negative consequences: from the original affirmative disjunction, negative disjunctions follow by virtue of the principle of contrariety (ʿinād) and implication. For example, 'Not at all is A either B or not C'. Next, the author discusses the universal negative disjunction. Some universal negatives entail the affirmative, and then inherit all the consequences of that affirmative; others do not entail anything. The author briefly mentions that the distinguishing feature of these will be explained later. The passage then applies these principles to the exclusive disjunction (māniʿa al-jamʿ) and the exclusive disjunction (māniʿa min al-khuluww) separately, providing examples with variables (J, B, D, H, Z). For each, it lists the affirmative consequences and the negative consequences. The commentary is essentially a catalog of derivable disjunctions, which are used in the logic of syllogisms (qiyāsāt).

المنفصلات الحقيقية
المانعة من الجمع والخلو
العناد

Negative Consequences and the Ambiguity of the Universal Negative Disjunction
Lists negative consequences from universal affirmative and discusses universal negative disjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive negative disjunctions from a universal affirmative inclusive disjunction and explain whether a universal negative disjunction entails affirmative conclusions.

Core Idea
Contrariety generates negative consequences; a universal negative disjunction sometimes entails the affirmative, sometimes not.

Having seen the affirmative consequences, we now turn to the negative ones. The author shows that from the same inclusive disjunction, we can derive negative disjunctions using the principle of contrariety (ʿinād). Additionally, the universal negative disjunction can be of two kinds: one that entails the affirmative (and then inherits its consequences) and one that does not. This distinction is crucial for understanding the behavior of disjunctions in proofs.

Guided Path
1
Recall the principle of contrariety and implication
If two things are contraries, then one implies the negation of the other. The text states: 'contrariety between a thing and another occurs only when implication holds between that thing and the negation of that other.' So from the original disjunction, we can derive negative consequences.
2
Derive a negative consequence from the affirmative
From 'Always every A is either B or C', we can say 'Not at all is A either B or not C'. Because if A were both B and not C, then it would not be C, contradicting the original.
There are multiple negative consequences mirroring the affirmative ones.
3
List additional negative consequences
Similarly, 'Not at all is A either B or the equal of the negation of C', 'Not at all is A either what is equal to the negation of B or the negation of C', etc.
The number of negative consequences equals the number of affirmative ones.
4
Examine the universal negative disjunction
A universal negative disjunction (e.g., 'Not at all is A either B or C') may entail the universal affirmative (i.e., 'Always every A is either B or C') or may not. If it does, then it also entails all the consequences of that affirmative. If it does not, it entails nothing else.
This distinction depends on the nature of the disjunction; more will be explained later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage is a systematic exposition of the logical consequences (lawāzim) that follow from universal affirmative and negative disjunctions, both inclusive (māniʿa al-jamʿ wa al-khuluww) and exclusive (māniʿa al-jamʿ and māniʿa min al-khuluww). The number of consequences depends on the multiplicity of these relations. Then, the author turns to the negative consequences: from the original affirmative disjunction, negative disjunctions follow by virtue of the principle of contrariety (ʿinād) and implication. For example, 'Not at all is A either B or not C'. Next, the author discusses the universal negative disjunction. Some universal negatives entail the affirmative, and then inherit all the consequences of that affirmative; others do not entail anything. The author briefly mentions that the distinguishing feature of these will be explained later. The passage then applies these principles to the exclusive disjunction (māniʿa al-jamʿ) and the exclusive disjunction (māniʿa min al-khuluww) separately, providing examples with variables (J, B, D, H, Z). For each, it lists the affirmative consequences and the negative consequences. The commentary is essentially a catalog of derivable disjunctions, which are used in the logic of syllogisms (qiyāsāt).

Key Terms
السوالب
العناد
السالبة الكلية المستلزمة
السالبة الكلية غير المستلزمة

Consequences from Exclusive Disjunctions (Māniʿa al-Jamʿ and Māniʿa min al-Khuluww)
Lists consequences from exclusive disjunctions (māniʿa al-jamʿ and māniʿa min al-khuluww)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive affirmative and negative consequences from exclusive disjunctions of both types: māniʿa al-jamʿ and māniʿa min al-khuluww.

Core Idea
Exclusive disjunctions, whether excluding both truth or both falsehood, also yield systematic consequences via negation and specificity.

The author concludes the discussion by applying the same methods to the other two types of disjunctions: the exclusive disjunction (māniʿa al-jamʿ) which prevents both being true, and the exclusive disjunction (māniʿa min al-khuluww) which prevents both being false. The consequences here involve not only negation and equality but also specificity (akhaṣṣ) and generality (aʿamm). This lesson equips you to handle all disjunctive forms in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two exclusive disjunctions
Māniʿa al-jamʿ: Cannot both be true but can both be false. Example: 'Every J is either B or D' with the condition that B and D cannot both be true. Māniʿa min al-khuluww: Cannot both be false but can both be true. Example: 'Every D is either H or not Z' where not Z is the negation of Z.
2
Derive consequences for māniʿa al-jamʿ
From 'Every J is either B or D' (māniʿa al-jamʿ), we derive: 'Every J is either B or not B' (a real disjunction), 'Every J is either B or what is more specific than D' (e.g., H is more specific than D), and similarly with what is more specific than B.
The derived disjunctions are real (munfaṣilāt ḥaqīqiyya).
3
Derive negative consequences for māniʿa al-jamʿ
Negative consequences include: 'Not at all is J either B or not D', 'Not at all is J either D or not B', etc.
4
Derive consequences for māniʿa min al-khuluww
From 'Every D is either H or not Z' (māniʿa min al-khuluww), we derive: 'Every D is either Z or not H', 'Every D is either H or not B' (where not B is more general than not Z), and similar. The disjunction in these is like the original.
Generality (aʿamm) is used instead of specificity.
5
Derive negative consequences for māniʿa min al-khuluww
Examples: 'Not at all is D either H or Z', 'Not at all is D either not H or not Z'.

موجبة وسالبة. وأما اللا مستلزمة فإنها لا تكون مستلزمة لا للموجبات ولا للسوالب لما مرّ من قبل في المنفصلات. ثمَّ المستلزمة منهما تعرف من بعد بأنها ما هي، وكيف هي، وكذلك اللا مستلزمة هذا هو الكلام في المانعة من الجمع والخلو. وأما في المانعة من الجمع فنقول : إذا صدق قولنا دائما كل «ج» إما «ب» وإما د»، صدق قولنا كل (ج) إما «ب» وإما «خ» غير «ب». وكذلك كل «ج» إما «د» وإما غير «د»، والانفصال فيهما As for the second inquiry: it is about the consequences (lawāzim) from the disjunctions for the universal affirmative, which have been mentioned previously, that is the inclusive and exclusive disjunction (māniʿa al-jamʿ wa al-khuluww), and for the universal negative likewise. As for the affirmative, we say: if our statement 'Always every A is either B or C' is true, then among the true disjunctions (al-munfaṣilāt al-ḥaqīqiyya) is what is composed of the very antecedent and the negation of B, so it is said 'Always every A is either B or not B'. Likewise, from the very antecedent and what is equal to the negation, and from the very antecedent and what is equal to C, and it is multiple necessarily according to the multiplicity of what is equal. And from what is equal to this. And likewise, it is composed from the very antecedent and what is contrary to B and it is multiple according to the multiplicity of contraries, and from the very antecedent and B itself, and it is multiple to the utmost. Likewise, it is composed from the requirement (muqtaḍā) of B and C, and from what is equal to this negation. And likewise. So these are the affirmative consequences. As for the negative ones, they are multiple according to the multiplicity of these affirmatives. For contrariety (ʿinād) does not exist between a thing and another unless implication is realized between it and the negation of that other, as has passed. And implication is among that which entails the truth of the negative, so it is said: 'Not at all is A either B or not C', and 'Not at all is A either B or the equal of the negation, i.e., the negation of C'. And so on for others. So these are the consequences for the universal affirmative. As for the universal negative, the universal negative may entail the universal affirmative or may not. As for what entails, just as it entails the affirmative, so it entails all that are among the consequences of the affirmative from the real and non-real disjunctions, affirmative and negative. As for what does not entail, it does not entail either affirmatives or negatives, as has preceded regarding disjunctions. Then what entails from these two is known later by what it is and how it is, and likewise what does not entail. This is the discussion regarding the inclusive and exclusive disjunction (māniʿa al-jamʿ wa al-khuluww). As for the exclusive disjunction (māniʿa al-jamʿ), we say: if our statement 'Always every J is either B or D' is true, then our statement 'Every J is either B or K other than B' is true. Likewise, 'Every J is either D or other than D', and the disjunction in them is a real disjunction. And also true is 'Every J is either B or H', where H is more specific (akhaṣṣ) than D, and likewise 'Every J is either D or Z', where Z is more specific than B. And the disjunction in them is similar to the disjunction in the original. So these are the consequences from affirmatives. As for the negatives, for example our statement: 'Not at all is J either B or not D', and 'Not at all is J either D or not B'. And similarly for the exclusive disjunction (māniʿa min al-khuluww): if our statement 'Every D is either H or not Z' is true, then our statement 'Every D is either Z or not H' is true. Likewise, 'Every D is either H or not B', where 'not B' is more general (aʿamm) than 'not Z', and the disjunction in it is similar to the disjunction in the original. As for what is from negatives, it is like our statement: 'Not at all is D either H or Z', and likewise 'Not at all is D either not H or not Z'.

Commentary
This passage is a systematic exposition of the logical consequences (lawāzim) that follow from universal affirmative and negative disjunctions, both inclusive (māniʿa al-jamʿ wa al-khuluww) and exclusive (māniʿa al-jamʿ and māniʿa min al-khuluww). The author first addresses the universal affirmative disjunction 'Every A is either B or C' (which is inclusive). From this, many other disjunctions necessarily follow, such as 'Every A is either B or not B', 'Every A is either B or what is equal to the negation of B', and so on. The author explains that these disjunctions are constructed from the antecedent and various related terms (like the negation, the equal, the contrary, etc.). The number of consequences depends on the multiplicity of these relations. Then, the author turns to the negative consequences: from the original affirmative disjunction, negative disjunctions follow by virtue of the principle of contrariety (ʿinād) and implication. For example, 'Not at all is A either B or not C'. Next, the author discusses the universal negative disjunction. Some universal negatives entail the affirmative, and then inherit all the consequences of that affirmative; others do not entail anything. The author briefly mentions that the distinguishing feature of these will be explained later. The passage then applies these principles to the exclusive disjunction (māniʿa al-jamʿ) and the exclusive disjunction (māniʿa min al-khuluww) separately, providing examples with variables (J, B, D, H, Z). For each, it lists the affirmative consequences and the negative consequences. The commentary is essentially a catalog of derivable disjunctions, which are used in the logic of syllogisms (qiyāsāt).

Immediate Inferences from Universal Exclusive Disjunctions
Author explains the logical consequences (lawazim) of universal disjunctions in exclusive disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the necessary consequences (lawazim) that follow from a universal exclusive disjunction and distinguish cases where consequences are or are not entailed.

Core Idea
Universal exclusive disjunctions necessitate specific consequences, but not all disjunctions imply the same.

In Arabic logic, the concept of lawazim (necessary consequences) is vital for valid inference. This passage examines how universal exclusive disjunctions – those where exactly one disjunct is true – yield specific necessary truths. Understanding these consequences sharpens your ability to derive valid conclusions from disjunctive premises, a common tool in Islamic juristic reasoning and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of universal disjunction
Determine whether the disjunction is affirmative (e.g., 'Every A is either B or C') or negative (e.g., 'No A is either B or C'). Also check if it is exclusive (mani'at al-jam' wal-khuluw) – meaning exactly one disjunct holds.
The passage focuses on exclusive disjunctions.
2
Extract the necessary consequences for the affirmative
If the disjunction is universal affirmative and exclusive, then its truth necessitates the truth of a universal negative: 'No A is either B or not C' (or equivalent). The author states this, then generalizes: the affirmative implies all consequences that follow from itself.
Think of it as: from 'Every A is either B or C' (exclusive), it follows that no A is B and not C (i.e., B and not C is impossible).
3
Analyze the universal negative case
The author says a universal negative exclusive disjunction may or may not necessitate the universal affirmative. If it does necessitate, then it also necessitates all the consequences that the affirmative would have. If it does not, it necessitates nothing – neither affirmatives nor negatives.
This asymmetry is crucial. Not all universal negatives are equally informative.
4
Apply the rule to an Islamic example
Consider a juristic ruling: 'Every contract is either valid (sahih) or invalid (fasid)' (exclusive disjunction). This is universal affirmative. Then it necessarily follows that 'No contract is both valid and invalid' – a negative consequence. Test this with real contracts.
Use the same method to verify other examples from usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وأما للسالبة الكلية فالسالبة الكلية قد تكون مستلزمة للموجبة الكلية وقد لا تكون أما المستلزمة فإنها كما هي مستلزمة للموجبة فكذلك لجميع ما يكون من اللوازم للموجبة من المنفصلات الحقيقية وغير الحقيقية موجبة وسالبة. وأما اللا مستلزمة فإنها لا تكون مستلزمة لا للموجبات ولا للسوالب لما مرّ من قبل في المنفصلات. ثمَّ المستلزمة منهما تعرف من بعد بأنها ما هي، وكيف هي، وكذلك اللا مستلزمة هذا هو الكلام في المانعة من الجمع والخلو.
The truth of the negative is necessitated, so it is said: Not at all 'A' is either 'B' or not 'C', and not at all is either 'B' or the equal of the negation, meaning the negation of 'J'. And likewise for the other: these are the necessities for the universal affirmative. As for the universal negative, the universal negative sometimes necessitates the universal affirmative and sometimes not. As for the necessitating, it just as it necessitates the affirmative, so too for all that are from the necessities of the affirmative among the real and non-real disjunctions, affirmative and negative. As for the non-necessitating, it does not necessitate either affirmatives or negatives, as was mentioned previously regarding disjunctions. Then the necessitating of the two is known afterwards by what it is and how it is, and likewise the non-necessitating. This is the discourse concerning the exclusive disjunction.

Commentary
This passage explains the logical consequences (lawazim) of universal exclusive disjunctions. The author begins with the universal affirmative: if 'Every A is either B or C' is true (exclusively), then it follows that 'No A is either B or not C' (since being B and not C is impossible). More generally, the universal affirmative implies a set of necessary consequences, each also universal affirmative. For the universal negative, it may or may not imply the universal affirmative; if it does, it implies all the same consequences as the affirmative. If it does not, it implies nothing. The author notes that this is the discussion concerning exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw). The key point is that the nature of the disjunction (exclusive) determines what follows.

Immediate Consequences from Exclusive Disjunctions: Mani' al-Jam' and Mani' al-Khuluw
Author explains necessary consequences for disjunctions that are only exclusive in one direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive valid necessary consequences from a given exclusive disjunction (either mani' al-jam' or mani' al-khuluw) by substituting more specific or more general terms, and identify which transformations preserve the disjunction's exclusivity type.

Core Idea
Exclusive disjunctions have predictable consequences—substituting a more specific term preserves truth in mani' al-jam', while swapping and generalizing works in mani' al-khuluw.

In Islamic logic, disjunctions are not just any 'or'—they come with specific exclusivity conditions: mani' al-jam' (cannot both be true) and mani' al-khuluw (cannot both be false). Understanding what follows necessarily from each type is crucial for constructing valid arguments in kalam and fiqh. This lesson breaks down the immediate consequences taught by the author, using the logical variables from the original text and applying them to Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exclusive disjunction type
Determine whether the disjunction is mani' al-jam' (prevents both disjuncts from being true) or mani' al-khuluw (prevents both from being false). This depends on the logical relationship between the disjuncts.
2
For mani' al-jam', derive a consequence by substituting a more specific term
Given a true universal affirmative disjunction 'Every S is either P or Q', if you replace Q with a term H that is more specific (a species) than Q, the new disjunction 'Every S is either P or H' is also true and remains mani' al-jam'. The same applies if you replace P with a more specific term under it.
The author uses the example: if 'Every J is either B or D' is true, then 'Every J is either B or H' where H is more specific than D is also true.
3
For mani' al-khuluw, derive consequences by swapping and generalizing
Given a true disjunction 'Every S is either P or not Q', two consequences follow: (1) 'Every S is either Q or not P' (swap both disjuncts and negate), and (2) replace the negative disjunct with a more general negative: 'Every S is either P or not R' where not R is more general than not Q.
The author illustrates: if 'Every D is either H or not Z' is true, then 'Every D is either Z or not H' is true, and also 'Every D is either H or not B' where not B is more general than not Z.
4
Contrast the two types
Note the difference: in mani' al-jam', you substitute with more specific terms; in mani' al-khuluw, you swap and substitute with more general terms. This distinction is essential for correct reasoning in Islamic legal and theological disputations.
A common mistake is to apply the mani' al-jam' rule to mani' al-khuluw cases, which would yield invalid consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في المانعة من الجمع فنقول : إذا صدق قولنا دائما كل «ج» إما «ب» وإما د»، صدق قولنا كل (ج) إما «ب» وإما «خ» غير «ب». وكذلك كل «ج» إما «د» وإما غير «د»، والانفصال فيهما انفصال حقيقي. وقد صدق أيضًا كل "ج " إما (ب) و إما (هـ)؛ و (هـ) أخص من «د»، وكذلك كل «ج» إما «د» وإما «ز»، و «ز» أخص من «ب». والانفصال فيهما نحو الانفصال في الأصل فهذه هي اللوازم من الموجبات. وأما للسوالب فمثل قولنا ليس البتة «ج» إما أن تكون «ب» وإما أن لا يكون «د»، وليس البتة إما أن تكون «د» وإما أن لا تكون «ب».
وعلى هذا في المانعة من الخلو ، فإنه إذا صدق قولنا كل «د» إما «ه» وإما لیس از صدق قولنا كل (د) إما (ز) وإما ليس (هـ). وكذلك إما «ه» وإما ليس «ب»، وليس (ب) أعم من ليس «ز»، والانفصال فيه نحو الانفصال في الأصل. وأما ما يكون من السوالب فمثل قولنا ليس البتة «د» إما «ه» وإما «ز»، وكذلك ليس البتة إما ليس (هـ) وإما ليس «ز».
As for the disjunction that prohibits conjunction, we say: if our saying is true 'Always every J is either B or D', then our saying 'Every J is either B or something other than B' is true. And likewise 'Every J is either D or not D', and the disjunction in both is real. Also 'Every J is either B or H', and H is more specific than D, and likewise 'Every J is either D or Z', and Z is more specific than B. And the disjunction in them is like the disjunction in the original. So these are the necessities from the affirmatives. As for the negatives, like our saying 'Not at all J is either B or not D', and 'Not at all is either D or not B'. And likewise regarding the disjunction that prohibits emptiness: if our saying 'Every D is either H or not Z' is true, then our saying 'Every D is either Z or not H' is true. And likewise 'Either H or not B', and 'not B' is more general than 'not Z', and the disjunction in it is like the disjunction in the original. And what is from the negatives is like our saying 'Not at all D is either H or Z', and likewise 'Not at all is either not H or not Z'.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage examines the logical consequences for two types of exclusive disjunctions: those that only prohibit conjunction (mani' al-jam') and those that only prohibit emptiness (mani' al-khuluw). For mani' al-jam', if a universal affirmative disjunction 'Every J is either B or D' is true, then it implies that 'Every J is either B or something other than B' (which is trivial) and also 'Every J is either D or not D'. More importantly, it implies 'Every J is either B or H' where H is a species under D, and 'Every J is either D or Z' where Z is a species under B. The disjunction in these followings is still a real disjunction (exclusive in the sense of mani' al-jam'). For the negative case, the truth of 'No J is either B or not D' (etc.) follows. For mani' al-khuluw, similar patterns obtain but with the opposite exclusivity. The author illustrates with examples using variables and notes the relationships of specificity. The key is that the type of exclusivity dictates which consequences are valid.

Key Terms
المانعة من الجمع (al-māniʿa min al-jamʿ): Disjunction that prohibits the conjunction of the two disjuncts (they cannot both be true), but allows both to be false. [Note: Standard term for one type of exclusive disjunction.]
المانعة من الخلو (al-māniʿa min al-khuluw): Disjunction that prohibits both disjuncts being false (at least one must be true), but allows both to be true. [Note: Standard term; contrast with mani' al-jam'.]
أخص (akhass): More specific; here, it indicates that one term is a species or subset of another (e.g., H is more specific than D). [Note: Standard logical relation; opposite of a'amm (more general).]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a mani' al-jam' disjunction, if 'Every contract is either valid (sahih) or invalid (batil)' is true, which of the following is a necessary consequence according to the passage?
A) Every contract is either valid or something more specific than invalid (e.g., voidable).
B) Every contract is either invalid or something more general than valid.
C) No contract is either valid or invalid.
D) Every contract is either valid or invalid, but not both.

Key Takeaway
For exclusive disjunctions, the type of exclusivity determines which term substitutions yield valid immediate consequences: specific-term substitutions hold for mani' al-jam', while swaps and general-term substitutions hold for mani' al-khuluw.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Mutual Entailment and Conversion of Disjunctives
Conditions for mutual entailment of disjunctives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two disjunctive propositions are mutually entailing and convertible, using the pattern of agreement in antecedent or consequent.

Core Idea
Disjunctive propositions are equivalent when they share agreement in antecedent or consequent with their negations.

In this passage, the author unpacks the conditions under which disjunctive (munfasil) propositions are mutually entailing (mutalāzima) and convertible (muta‘ākisa). This is an immediate inference—no external premises are needed—just a transformation of the proposition. The examples show that for universals and particulars, affirmatives and negatives, the equivalence holds if the disjuncts align in a certain pattern with their negations. This is a powerful tool for simplifying and verifying logical arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
In each disjunctive proposition, locate the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī). For example, in 'Always every A is either B or C', B is the antecedent and C is the consequent.
2
Check the pattern of agreement
Two disjunctives are mutually entailing if they agree either in the antecedent (with different consequents) or in the consequent (with different antecedents), and their negations follow the same pattern.
The text says: 'they are not free from being in agreement in the antecedent without the consequent, or in the consequent without the antecedent.'
3
Apply the pattern to universal affirmatives
For universal affirmatives, the pattern is: 'Always every A is either B or C' is equivalent to 'Not at all not every A is either B or C'. This holds even if C is replaced by D, as long as the antecedent B is fixed.
See the list of examples: 'Always every A is either B or D' paired with its negation.
4
Apply the pattern to particulars
For particulars, the pattern changes slightly: 'Always every A is either D or J' is equivalent to 'Not at all not every A is either D or J'. The reverse also works: 'Sometimes every A is either D or J' is equivalent to 'Not always no A is either D or J'.
The author distinguishes universal and particular cases.
5
Generalize to affirmatives and negatives
The author concludes that all affirmative disjunctives (universal or particular) are mutually entailing and convertible with their negations, and the same for negative disjunctives.
This is a sweeping claim—test it with your own examples.

Section: On explaining what among disjunctives are mutually entailing and convertible, whether affirmative or negative, universal or particular. That is because they are not free from being in agreement in the antecedent without the consequent, or in the consequent without the antecedent, or not being, but being in agreement in this without that, or the reverse. And those differing in the two forms, if they are mutually entailing and convertible, then all disjunctives are mutually entailing and convertible according to this example: Always every A is either B or C, not at all not every A is either B or C. Always every A is either B or D, not at all not every A is either B or D. Always every A is either B or H, not at all not every A is either B or H. Always every A is either B or W, not at all not every A is either B or W. Always every A is either B or Z, not at all not every A is either B or Z. Always every A is either B or H, not at all not every A is either B or H. And likewise if the matter is reversed, as is said: Always every A is either B or J, not at all not every A is either B or J. Always every A is either D or J, not at all not every A is either D or J. Always every A is either H or J, not at all not every A is either H or J. Always every A is either W or J, not at all not every A is either W or J. Always every A is either Z or J, not at all not every A is either Z or J. Always every A is either H or J, not at all not every A is either H or J. So all affirmatives are mutually entailing and convertible, and likewise the negatives. As for particulars, it is said: Always every A is either D or J, not at all not every A is either D or J. Always every A is either W or J, not at all not every A is either W or J. Always every A is either H or J, not at all not every A is either H or J. And likewise if the matter is reversed, as is said: Sometimes every A is either D or J, not always no A is either D or J. Sometimes every A is either W or J, not always no A is either W or J. Sometimes every A is either H or J, not always no A is either H or J. So the affirmatives among these particulars are mutually entailing and convertible, and likewise the negatives. Then, in this amount of mentioning the necessary between conditionals is sufficient; for encompassing that benefits encompassing all that is of the necessary things in knowledge in this type. As for consideration of truth and falsity, that is according to the rules that were mentioned previously in this.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage explains when disjunctive propositions (munfasilāt) are mutually entailing (mutalāzima) and convertible (muta‘ākisa). The author first states the general condition: disjunctives can agree in the antecedent (muqaddam) without the consequent (tālī), or in the consequent without the antecedent, or not agree in these ways, or do the opposite. When two disjunctives differ in form but are mutually entailing and convertible, then all such disjunctives share that property. The author then provides examples for universal affirmatives: e.g., 'Always every A is either B or C' is equivalent to 'Not at all not every A is either B or C' (the negation of the negative). Similarly, with varying consequents (D, H, etc.) while keeping the antecedent (B) fixed, the pattern holds. Then the reverse pattern is shown: keeping the consequent (J) fixed and varying the antecedents. Then the author states that all affirmative universal disjunctives are mutually entailing and convertible, and similarly for negatives. Next, particular disjunctives are treated. For particulars, the pattern is: 'Always every A is either D or J' is equivalent to 'Not at all not every A is either D or J' (negation of the particular negative). The reverse also holds: 'Sometimes every A is either D or J' is equivalent to 'Not always no A is either D or J'. Again, affirmatives and negatives among particulars are mutually entailing and convertible. The author concludes that this coverage of conditionals (muttaṣilāt) is sufficient for understanding all necessary relations in this type, and truth/falsity is determined by earlier rules. The passage uses variable letters (أ, ب, ج, د, etc.) as placeholders, which is standard in logical notation. The key insight is that mutual entailment and conversion of disjunctives depend on the pattern of agreement in antecedent or consequent.

Mutual Implication and Contraposition in Quantified Conditionals
Author explains mutual implication and contraposition in quantified conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply mutual implication and contraposition in quantified conditional propositions from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Quantified conditionals have built-in equivalences allowing immediate inference.

In this passage, the author shows that quantified conditionals—both universal and particular—have a mutual implicative and contrapositive relationship. This means that from a true conditional, we can immediately derive another proposition without additional premises. Understanding this is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology, where conditionals are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Recognize that the author uses disjunctive conditionals (إما...وإما) with quantifiers. The form is 'Every A is either D or J' or 'Some A is either D or J'.
2
Distinguish universal from particular
Universal conditionals use 'دائما' (always) and particular use 'قد يكون' (it may be). This affects the logical equivalence.
3
Determine the equivalent form
For a universal positive conditional, the author gives a negated form: 'ليس البتة ليس كل أ إما د وإما ج' (not at all is it not the case that every A is either D or J). This is the contrapositive or mutual implicant.
4
Apply the rule to Islamic examples
Substitute the variables with real Islamic content. For example, let A = 'sale', D = 'valid', J = 'invalid'. Then 'Every sale is either valid or invalid' is a universal conditional. Its contrapositive is 'Not at all is it not the case that every sale is either valid or invalid'—which is trivial, but the principle holds.
5
Check mutual implication for particulars
For particulars, the author shows that 'قد يكون كل أ إما د وإما ج' (it may be that every A is either D or J) is equivalent to 'ليس دائما لا شيء من أ إما د وإما ج' (it is not always that no A is either D or J). Verify this using negation rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالموجبات كلها متلازمة ومتعاكسة والسالبات كذلك، وأما في الجزئيات فيقال:
دائما كل «أ» إما «د» وإما «ج» ليس البتة ليس كل «أ» إما «د» وإما «ج»

All positives are mutually implicative and contrapositive, and likewise for negatives. As for particulars, it is said: Always every 'A' is either 'D' or 'J' — not at all is it not the case that every 'A' is either 'D' or 'J'. Always every 'A' is either 'W' or 'J' — not at all is it not the case that every 'A' is either 'W' or 'J'. Always every 'A' is either 'H' or 'J' — not at all is it not the case that every 'A' is either 'H' or 'J'. It may be that every 'A' is either 'H' or 'J' — it is not always the case that no 'A' is either 'H' or 'J'. So the positives among these particulars are mutually implicative and contrapositive, and likewise for the negatives. Then this amount of mention of the necessary among the conjunctives is sufficient; indeed, complete knowledge of that provides complete knowledge of all necessities that occur in this type.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage examines the logical relationships of mutual implication (talazum) and contraposition (ta'ākus) in conditional propositions (shartiyyāt), specifically quantified ones. The author begins by stating a general rule: all positive propositions are mutually implicative and contrapositive, and similarly for negative propositions. He then turns to particular (juz'iyyāt) cases, giving examples with variable letters (A, D, J, W, H). The first set of examples shows that if a universal conditional is true, its negation (using 'laysa al-battah') is false; this illustrates mutual implication. The second set shows that the partial converse (using 'qad yakūn') corresponds to the negation of a universal negative. The author concludes by noting that this discussion suffices for understanding the necessary relations in conjunctives. For instance, in Islamic legal reasoning, if a universally quantified conditional is true, such as 'Every khamr (intoxicant) is forbidden,' then its contrapositive is also true: 'Whatever is not forbidden is not khamr.' This demonstrates the contraposition of conditionals.

The Effect of Implication and Opposition on Conditional Truth Values
Definition of truth effects of implication and opposition in conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of a conditional proposition based on whether its components are related by implication or opposition.

Core Idea
Implication and opposition flip truth conditions between connected and disjunctive conditionals.

This lesson unpacks a key passage from classical logic that defines how the relations of mulāzama (implication) and 'inād (opposition) govern the truth of conditional propositions. Understanding these rules is essential for evaluating conditional arguments in Islamic sciences, such as in uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Determine whether the proposition is a connected conditional (muttasila: 'if A then B') or a disjunctive conditional (munfaşila: 'either A or B'). This is the first step because the truth rules differ.
Connected conditionals assert a link; disjunctives assert exclusivity.
2
Determine the relation between the components
Check whether the two parts are related by implication (mulāzama: if A is true, B must be true) or by opposition ('inād: A and B cannot both be true together). This relation is given or must be inferred from the context.
Implication means A necessitates B; opposition means A and B are mutually exclusive.
3
Apply the rule for affirmative conditionals
If the relation is implication: the connected conditional is true, the disjunctive is false. If the relation is opposition: the connected conditional is false, the disjunctive is true.
This is the core of the passage for mūjibāt (affirmatives).
4
Apply the inverse rule for negative conditionals
For negative conditionals (sālibāt), the truth values are reversed: implication makes the connected false and the disjunctive true; opposition makes the connected true and the disjunctive false.
The passage states that the rule is 'on the contrary' (على عكس ذلك) for negatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل وهو أن يعتبر كون الشيء مستلزما للشيء فإنه مما يستلزم الصدق في الموجبات من المتصلات، والكذب في الموجبات من المنفصلات: وإن يعتبر كونه منافيًا للشيء أيضًا فإنه مما يستلزم الكذب في المتصلات، والصدق في المنفصلات على عكس ذلك، وكذلك على العكس في السالبات.
The chapter: That one considers a thing being implying a thing — then it necessitates truth in the affirmatives of connected [conditionals], and falsehood in the affirmatives of disjunctive [conditionals]. And if one considers it being contrary to a thing also, then it necessitates falsehood in the connected [conditionals], and truth in the disjunctive [conditionals] in the opposite of that, and likewise the opposite in the negatives.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This is a definitional passage that establishes the fundamental relationship between logical implication (al-mulazama) and opposition (al-'inad) in conditional propositions. The author divides conditionals into two types: connected (mutasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). He states that if a proposition is considered as implying another, then the implication yields truth in the affirmative connected conditional (i.e., 'if A then B' is true when implication holds) and falsehood in the affirmative disjunctive conditional (i.e., 'either A or B' is false when A implies B, because if A implies B, then it is not the case that only one is true). Conversely, if a proposition is considered as being contrary to another (opposition), then it yields falsehood in the connected conditional and truth in the disjunctive conditional, as opposition implies that both cannot be true together, which is the opposite of implication. The same rule applies to negative propositions (salibat) in a reversed manner. This is a precise logical definition that governs the truth conditions of conditional statements based on the relations between their components.

Key Terms
ملازمة
عناد
متصلات
منفصلات
موجبات
سالبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Undetermined
D) Depends on material

Key Takeaway
The truth values of conditional propositions depend systematically on whether the components are related by implication or opposition, and the effect reverses between connected and disjunctive conditionals.

The Five Modal Layers and Their Necessary Consequences
Author enumerates five modal layers and specifies their necessary consequences and contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five modal layers of propositions and list the necessary consequences that follow from each via conversion.

Core Idea
Every modal layer has a set of necessary consequences derived by conversion; the contradictory of any middle layer subsumes the other two.

This passage presents the five layers of modal propositions—ranging from 'necessary to be' to 'not possible to be'—and explains what logically follows from each. Understanding these layers is crucial for manipulating modal statements in Islamic logic, particularly in kalam and usul al-fiqh. We will walk through each layer, its consequences, and how the contradictory and general possibility relate across layers.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the five modal layers
Identify the five layers as listed by the author: (1) ليس بواجب أن يكون, (2) واجب أن لا يكون, (3) ليس بواجب أن لا يكون, (4) ممكن أن يكون, (5) ليس بممكن أن يكون. Notice that layers 1, 3, and 5 have no additional consequences beyond conversion.
Layers 2 and 4 are the ones that have explicit lists of necessary consequences.
2
Extract the necessary consequences for layers 2 and 4
For 'واجب أن لا يكون' (necessary not to be), the consequences are: general possibility that it is not (ممكن العامي أن لا يكون), not specific possibility that it is (ليس بممكن الخاصي أن يكون), and not specific possibility that it is not (ليس بممكن الخاصي أن لا يكون). For 'ممكن أن يكون' (possible to be), the consequences are: not necessary to be, not necessary not to be, to be, not impossible not to be, and general possibility that it is not.
The author uses 'يلزمها' to indicate these follow necessarily.
3
Understand the role of conversion (انعكاس)
The author notes that for some layers (1, 3, 5), nothing is necessary except what is converted to them. Conversion here means transforming the proposition into an equivalent form. For instance, 'ليس بواجب أن يكون' converts to 'ممكن أن لا يكون'? The passage does not specify the conversion, but it hints that each layer has a converse that is necessary.
This mirrors the conversion rules for categorical propositions.
4
Analyze the contradictory relationship
The author states that the contradictory of each of the three middle layers (1, 2, 3? Actually, the text says 'each of the three layers' but from context it refers to the first three? Or maybe the middle three? The passage: 'ثم النقيض في كل طبقة من الطبقات الثلاث يحتوي على الطبقتين الباقيتين'. This means the contradictory of any one of the three layers contains the other two, making it the most general. For example, the contradictory of 'واجب أن لا يكون' is 'ليس بواجب أن لا يكون'? Actually, the contradictory of 'واجب أن لا يكون' is 'ليس بواجب أن لا يكون'? Wait, need to check: In modal logic, the contradictory of 'necessary P' is 'possible not P'. But here the layers are defined differently. The author is likely referring to the three layers that are not the extremes (not necessary to be, necessary not to be, not necessary not to be). The contradictory of one contains the other two as necessary consequences.
This is a key insight: contradictions are richer in information.
5
Relate general possibility (ممكن عامي) to the layers
The author concludes that general possibility is a necessary consequence that can be affirmative or negative depending on the layer. For the first layer (ليس بواجب أن يكون), it is affirmative (ممكن عامي أن يكون is not stated explicitly but implied). For the second layer (واجب أن لا يكون), it is negative (ممكن عامي أن لا يكون). For the third layer (ليس بواجب أن لا يكون), it appears both affirmatively and negatively? The text says 'معدولا ومحصلا للثالثة' meaning it is both affirmative and negative for the third. This parallels how possibility functions in categorical modal logic.
This shows the systematic nature of modal entailments.

وأما طبقة ليس بواجب أن يكون فلا يلزمها شيء غير ما ينعكس عليها؛ وأما طبقة واجب أن لا يكون فيلزمها: ممكن العامي أن لا يكون ليس بممكن الخاصي أن يكون ليس بممكن الخاصي أن لا يكون وأما طبقة ليس بواجب أن لا يكون فلا يلزمها شيء غير ما ينعكس عليها؛ وأما طبقة ممكن أن يكون فيلزمها: ليس بواجب أن يكون ليس بواجب أن لا يكون أن يكون ليس بممتنع أن لا يكون ممكن العامي أن لا يكون وأما طبقة ليس بممكن أن يكون فلا يلزمها شيء بطريق الانعكاس. ثم النقيض في كل طبقة من الطبقات الثلاث يحتوي على الطبقتين الباقيتين؛ فيكون هو الأعم من اللوازم بالنسبة إلى كل واحدة منهما. وكذا الممكن العامي من اللوازم محصلا للأولى، ومعدولا للثانية، ومعدولا ومحصلا للثالثة كما في الحمليّات.
And as for the layer 'not necessary to be', nothing is necessary for it other than what is converted to it; and as for the layer 'necessary not to be', the following are necessary for it: general possibility that it is not, not specific possibility that it is, not specific possibility that it is not. And as for the layer 'not necessary not to be', nothing is necessary for it other than what is converted to it; and as for the layer 'possible to be', the following are necessary for it: not necessary to be, not necessary not to be, to be, not impossible not to be, general possibility that it is not. And as for the layer 'not possible to be', nothing is necessary for it by way of conversion. Likewise the general possibility is among the necessary consequences: affirmative for the first, negative for the second, and both negative and affirmative for the third, as in the categoricals.

Commentary
This passage defines the five layers of modalities and their necessary consequences through conversion (انعكاس). The author lists each modal layer: (1) ليس بواجب أن يكون (not necessary to be), (2) واجب أن لا يكون (necessary not to be), (3) ليس بواجب أن لا يكون (not necessary not to be), (4) ممكن أن يكون (possible to be), (5) ليس بممكن أن يكون (not possible to be). For each, he specifies what follows from it. For example, from 'necessary not to be' it follows that it is generally possible that it is not (i.e., not impossible), and that it is not specifically possible that it is (i.e., not contingent), and that it is not specifically possible that it is not (i.e., not necessary not to be). The author then notes that the contradictory of any of the three middle layers (excluding the extremes) contains the other two, meaning it is the most general. Finally, he discusses the 'general possibility' (ممكن عامي) as a necessary consequence that can be affirmative or negative depending on the layer. This mirrors the earlier discussion on categorical propositions.

Converting Negative to Positive Conditionals: The Rule of Preservation
Author explains rule for converting negative to positive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rule from a universal negative conditional to a universal affirmative conditional and identify the preserved quantity and changed quality.

Core Idea
Every negative conditional implies an affirmative by preserving quantity and negating the consequent.

In this passage, the author reveals a powerful conversion rule: every universal negative conditional (like 'It is never the case that if A then B') necessarily implies a corresponding universal affirmative conditional ('Whenever A, not B'). This is not simply contraposition; it is an immediate inference that changes the quality of the consequent while keeping the antecedent untouched. Understanding this rule is essential for manipulating conditional propositions in logical arguments, especially in Islamic theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original negative conditional
Find the universal negative conditional in the text. It has the form 'ليس البتة إذا كان كل أب فكل ج د' (It is absolutely not the case that if every A is B then every C is D).
The original proposition is a universal negative conditional (saliba kulliyya).
2
Apply the conversion rule
Keep the antecedent unchanged (universal) and replace the consequent with its negation. The result is 'كلما كان كل أب فليس كل ج د' (Whenever every A is B, then not every C is D). The quantity is preserved, but the quality of the consequent is changed from affirmative to negative.
Preserve quantity (universal/particular) but change quality (affirmative/negative) of the consequent.
3
Check the mode: absolute vs. necessary
The conversion holds whether the connection is absolute (ittisal mutlaq) or necessary (luzum). In absolute connection, the consequent follows in fact; in necessary connection, it follows necessarily. Both produce the same affirmative form but with different modal strength.
The word 'implication' (luzum) is used by the author to cover both cases.
4
Verify with the proof by contradiction
The author proves the implication is necessary because if it were not, then the same proposition would both imply and not imply its affirmative counterpart, which is absurd. This proof confirms the validity of the conversion.
The proof is from earlier in the text; it ensures the inference is truth-preserving.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لكل نوع من الأنواع السالبة الكلية لازم من الأنواع الموجبة الكلية ويرجع بعضها إلى البعض، ووجه الرجوع أن تحفظ الكمية دون الكيفية، ويحفظ المقدم كما هو، ويمنع بنقيض التالي؛ وجميعها يوجد تارة باتصال مطلق وتارةً باتصال ولزوم فيراد لفظة اللزوم. فقولنا ليس البتة إذا كان كل «أب» فكل «ج د»، وهو على المعنى الأعم في قوة قولنا كلما كان كل «أب» فليس كل «ج د»؛ وفي معنى الاتصال واللزوم في قوة قولنا كلَّما كان كل «أب» فليس كل جد»، واحفظ هذا القانون في جميع ذلك. وأما وجه البرهان على هذا اللزوم فقد مرَّ من قبل أنه من اللوازم وإلا لكان الشيء لازما للشيء ولا يكون لازما أيضًا، هذا خلف. وكما أن الموجبة من لوازم السالبة فكذلك السالبة للموجبة تارةً بالاتصال المطلق وتارة باللزوم؛ وذلك يعرف بالبرهان الذي مر ذكره، وبغير ذلك أيضًا إن تأملت فيه. وبالله التوفيق.
Then for every type of universal negative proposition there is a necessary implication from the types of universal affirmative propositions, and some of them revert to others. The manner of reversion is that you preserve the quantity without the quality, and you preserve the antecedent as it is, and you negate the consequent. All of them exist sometimes with absolute connection and sometimes with connection and implication, so the word 'implication' should be used. So our statement 'It is absolutely not the case that if every Ab then every Jd' is, in the more general sense, equivalent to our statement 'Whenever every Ab, then not every Jd'; and in the sense of connection and implication, it is equivalent to our statement 'Whenever every Ab, then not every Jd'. And memorize this rule in all of that. As for the proof of this implication, it has already been mentioned that it is from the necessary implications, otherwise a thing would be necessary for a thing and also not necessary, which is absurd. And just as the affirmative is from the implications of the negative, so too the negative for the affirmative sometimes by absolute connection and sometimes by implication; and that is known by the proof mentioned, and by other means if you reflect. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage explains the logical relationship between negative and affirmative conditionals. The author states that every universal negative conditional has a corresponding universal affirmative conditional as a necessary implication (lazim). The conversion rule is: keep the quantity (i.e., universal or particular) of the antecedent unchanged, change the quality of the consequent from affirmative to negative (or vice versa), and negate the entire consequent. The author distinguishes between absolute connection (ittisal mutlaq) and necessary connection (luzum). The proof is by contradiction: if the implication were not necessary, then the same proposition would both imply and not imply, which is impossible. The rule applies to both absolute and necessary connections.

Interpreting Necessary Implications (Lawazim) of Categorical Propositions
Enumerates the necessary implications between categorical propositions in conditional form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a conditional implication between categorical propositions is necessary ('laysa al-batta') or possible ('qad yakun') by applying the rule of preserving quantity and negating the quality of the consequent.

Core Idea
The necessary implications between categorical propositions are expressed as conditionals where impossible configurations are excluded.

This passage from a classical logic text systematizes the relationships between categorical propositions using conditional forms. You will learn to recognize the two types of implication expressions—'laysa al-batta' (absolutely not) and 'qad yakun' (it may be)—and understand the underlying rule that allows you to generate these implications: preserve the quantity of the antecedent but flip its quality in the consequent. This is a key tool for immediate inference.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of conditionals
Notice the expressions 'ليس البتة' (absolutely not) and 'قد يكون' (it may be). The first indicates that the conditional is necessarily true (impossible for antecedent true and consequent false). The second indicates possibility (the conditional is not impossible but not necessarily true either). Identify which type is used for universal propositions and which for particulars in the passage.
The passage lists universal implications under 'ليس البتة' and particular under 'قد يكون'.
2
Extract the reduction rule
The author states: 'وجه الرجوع أن تحفظ الكمية دون الكيفية، ويحفظ المقدم كما هو، ويمنع بنقيض التالي' (the mode of reduction is that you preserve the quantity without the quality, you preserve the antecedent as it is, and you prevent the contradictory of the consequent). Apply this rule to the example given: 'ليس البتة إذا كان كل (أ ب) فكل (ج د)' becomes in the sense of implication 'كلما كان كل (أ ب) فليس كل (ج د)'. Note how quantity (universal) is preserved, but quality changes: the consequent becomes negative because you prevent its contradictory (which would be 'كل (ج د)' affirmative).
3
Verify the proof by contradiction
The author argues that if the implication were not necessary, a thing could be necessary for another without actually being necessary, which is absurd. This is a reductio: assume the implication holds but is not necessary, then consider a case where antecedent true and consequent false—that would violate the necessary relationship. Confirm that this reasoning aligns with the logical definition of implication.
This step helps internalize why the implications are necessary.
4
Practice with a specific implication pair
Choose one of the listed 'ليس البتة' conditionals and one of the 'قد يكون' conditionals. Write them out using real Islamic science terms (e.g., A = 'thing is an intoxicant', B = 'thing is khamr', C = 'thing is forbidden', D = 'thing is haram'). Verify that your instantiation follows the same pattern and the rule holds.
This bridges abstract variables to concrete Islamic examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنظومة
ليس البتة إذا كان بعض (أ ب) فكل (ج د)
ليس البتة إذا كان بعض (أ ب) فبعض (ج د)
ليس البتة إذا كان لا شيء من (أ ب) فلا شيء من (ج د)
وعلى هذا إلى آخر ما مر من القضايا الموجبة الكلية ثم لكل نوع من الأنواع السالبة الكلية لازم من الأنواع الموجبة الكلية ويرجع بعضها إلى البعض، ووجه الرجوع أن تحفظ الكمية دون الكيفية، ويحفظ المقدم كما هو، ويمنع بنقيض التالي؛ وجميعها يوجد تارة باتصال مطلق وتارةً باتصال ولزوم فيراد لفظة اللزوم. فقولنا ليس البتة إذا كان كل (أ ب) فكل (ج د)، وهو على المعنى الأعم في قوة قولنا كلما كان كل (أ ب) فليس كل (ج د)؛ وفي معنى الاتصال واللزوم في قوة قولنا كلَّما كان كل (أ ب) فليس كل (ج د)، واحفظ هذا القانون في جميع ذلك. وأما وجه البرهان على هذا اللزوم فقد مرَّ من قبل أنه من اللوازم وإلا لكان الشيء لازما للشيء ولا يكون لازما أيضًا، هذا خلف. وكما أن الموجبة من لوازم السالبة فكذلك السالبة للموجبة تارةً بالاتصال المطلق وتارة باللزوم؛ وذلك يعرف بالبرهان الذي مر ذكره، وبغير ذلك أيضًا إن تأملت فيه. وبالله التوفيق.

ثم وأما الجزئيات فعلى الترتيب الذي مرَّ ذكره من قبل فهذه:
قد يكون إذا كان كل (أ ب) فكل (ج د)
قد يكون إذا كان كل (أ ب) فبعض (ج د)
قد يكون إذا كان بعض (أ ب) فكل (ج د)
قد يكون إذا كان بعض (أ ب) فبعض (ج د)
قد يكون إذا كان لا شيء من (أ ب) فلا كل (ج د)
قد يكون إذا كان لا كل (أ ب) فلا شيء من (ج د)
قد يكون إذا كان بعض (أ ب) فلا شيء من (ج د)
قد يكون إذا كان بعض (أ ب) فلا كل (ج د)
قد يكون إذا كان لا كل (أ ب) فكل (ج د)
قد يكون إذا كان لا كل (أ ب) فبعض (ج د)

وأما السوالب فعلى هذا الترتيب أيضًا:
ليس كلَّما كان كل (أ ب) فبعض (ج د)
ليس كلَّما كان بعض (أ ب) فبعض (ج د)
The System
Absolutely not: if some A is B, then every C is D.
Absolutely not: if some A is B, then some C is D.
Absolutely not: if no A is B, then no C is D.
And upon this to the end of what has passed of universal affirmative propositions; then for each type of universal negative proposition there is a necessary consequence from the universal affirmative types, and some of them reduce to others. The mode of reduction is that you preserve the quantity without the quality, and you preserve the antecedent as it is, and you prevent the contradictory of the consequent. All of them occur sometimes with absolute conjunction and sometimes with conjunction of implication, so the word 'luzum' is intended. So our saying 'Absolutely not: if every A is B, then every C is D' is, in the broader sense, equivalent to our saying 'Whenever every A is B, then not every C is D'. And in the sense of conjunction and implication, it is equivalent to our saying 'Whenever every A is B, then not every C is D'. Preserve this rule in all of that. As for the proof of this implication, it has passed before that it is among the necessary consequences; otherwise a thing would be necessary for another thing and yet not be necessary, which is absurd. And just as the affirmative is among the consequences of the negative, so also the negative is for the affirmative sometimes with absolute conjunction and sometimes with implication. That is known by the proof mentioned, and also by other means if you reflect on it. And with God is success.

Then as for the particulars, according to the arrangement that passed before, these are:
It may be: if every A is B, then every C is D.
It may be: if every A is B, then some C is D.
It may be: if some A is B, then every C is D.
It may be: if some A is B, then some C is D.
It may be: if no A is B, then not every C is D.
It may be: if not every A is B, then no C is D.
It may be: if some A is B, then no C is D.
It may be: if some A is B, then not every C is D.
It may be: if not every A is B, then every C is D.
It may be: if not every A is B, then some C is D.

As for the negatives, also according to this arrangement:
Not whenever every A is B, then some C is D.
Not whenever some A is B, then some C is D.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage defines the necessary implications (lawazim) between categorical propositions expressed as conditional statements. It is a systematic enumeration following the rules of the square of opposition and immediate inference. The author first lists three 'absolutely not' (laysa al-batta) patterns for universal propositions: from a particular affirmative antecedent to a universal consequent, from particular affirmative to particular, and from universal negative to universal negative. Then he explains the general principle: the reduction (ruju') involves preserving quantity (kammiyya) without preserving quality (kayfiyya), keeping the antecedent, and negating the consequent. This is essentially the rule of contraposition. The implications can be taken in two senses: absolute conjunction (ittisal mutlaq) and conjunction of implication (ittisal luzum). The example given shows that 'Absolutely not: if every A is B, then every C is D' is equivalent in the sense of implication to 'Whenever every A is B, then not every C is D'. The proof is provided by a reductio ad absurdum: if the implication were not necessary, then a thing could be a necessary condition for another without actually being necessary, which is contradictory. The passage then lists ten 'it may be' (qad yakun) patterns for particular propositions, covering affirmative and negative antecedents and consequents. Finally, two 'not whenever' (laysa kullama) patterns are given for negative particulars. This classification is essential for understanding hypothetical syllogisms and the relationships between categorical propositions in the logical tradition.

Parsing Conditional Quantifier Possibilities
Author enumerates valid possibilities for conditionals with universal/particular opposites and shows reductio proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the valid quantifier pairs for conditional propositions and reconstruct the reductio proof of implication.

Core Idea
Conditionals mirror categorical quantifier patterns across antecedent and consequent.

In this passage, the author teaches you the possible quantifier combinations in conditional propositions. Just as categorical statements have four types (A, E, I, O), conditionals also follow a structured pattern. We will parse the six affirmative possibilities and the negative ones, and then see a clever reductio proof that one conditional implies another.

Guided Path
1
Identify antecedent and consequent quantifiers
Read the six affirmative possibilities in the Arabic. For each, note the quantifier on the subject of the antecedent and the quantifier on the subject of the consequent. For example: 'if none of A is B' is universal negative ('لا شيء') in the antecedent; 'not every C is D' is particular negative in the consequent.
The quantifiers are: none (لا شيء), not every (لا كل), some (بعض), and every (كل).
2
List the six affirmative patterns
The passage gives: (1) none → not every; (2) not every → none; (3) some → none; (4) some → not every; (5) not every → every; (6) not every → some. Write them down and notice which quantifier pairs are excluded (e.g., none → every or every → every are not listed).
These correspond to the six valid conditional possibilities according to the author.
3
Understand the negative conditionals
The passage then states that the negatives ('سوالب') follow the same order. It gives two examples: 'Not every time that every A is B, then some C is D' and 'Not every time that some A is B, then some C is D'. The negation applies to the entire conditional, not just the consequent.
This shows that each affirmative possibility has a negative counterpart.
4
Reconstruct the reductio proof
The author asserts that 'not every time that every A is B then every C is D' implies 'it may be that if every A is B then not every C is D'. To prove this, assume the opposite: the implication fails. Then its contradictory holds, leading to a contradiction with the original statement. This proves the implication.
This is a standard proof by contradiction (khalf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد يكون إذا كان لا شيء من (أب) فلا كل جد قد يكون إذا كان لا كل «أب» فلا شيء من جده قد يكون إذا كان بعض أب) فلا شيء من (ج) د) قد يكون إذا كان بعض أب» فلا كل جد» قد يكون إذا كان لا كل «أب» فكل «ج د» قد يكون إذا كان لا كل «أب» فبعض «ج د» وأما السوالب فعلى هذا الترتيب أيضًا : ليس كلَّما كان كل «أب» فبعض «ج د» ليس كلَّما كان بعض «أب» فبعض «ج د» وعلي هذا إلى آخر ما مرَّ من الجزئيات. ثمَّ الحال في تلازم هذه الجزئيات نحو الحال في تلازم الكليات، فإن قولنا ليس كلَّما كان كل فكل يلزمه قولنا قد يكون إذا كان كل فليس كل وإلا لزم نقيضه وهو أنه اليس البتة إذا كان كل فليس»، فيلزمه أنه كلما كان كل فكل وقد قلنا ليس «كلما كان كل فكل»، هذا خلف.
It may be that if none of A is B, then not every C is D. It may be that if not every A is B, then no C is D. It may be that if some A is B, then no C is D. It may be that if some A is B, then not every C is D. It may be that if not every A is B, then every C is D. It may be that if not every A is B, then some C is D. As for the negatives, they follow this order also: Not every time that every A is B, then some C is D. Not every time that some A is B, then some C is D. And so on to the end of the particulars mentioned. Then the situation regarding the implication of these particulars is like the situation regarding the implication of the universals. For our saying 'not every time that every A is B, then every C is D' implies our saying 'it may be if every A is B, then not every C is D'; otherwise its contradictory would follow, which is 'it is not at all that if every A is B, then not every C is D', which implies that every time every A is B then every C is D, and we have said 'not every time that every A is B then every C is D'. This is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage presents the valid possibilities of conditional (shartiyya) propositions involving universal and particular terms (A, B, C, D as placeholders). It first lists six affirmative possibilities where the antecedent and consequent have various combinations of 'none', 'not all', and 'some'. Then it notes that negative possibilities follow the same pattern, giving two examples: 'Not every time that every A is B, then some C is D' and 'Not every time that some A is B, then some C is D'. The author then states that the implications among these particular conditional statements are analogous to the implications among the universal (kulliyya) categorical propositions. He illustrates with a reductio argument: the statement 'Not every time that every A is B, then every C is D' implies 'It may be if every A is B, then not every C is D'. If it did not imply this, the contradictory would hold, leading to the original statement being false. This shows that the logical relationships of subalternation and opposition carry over from categorical to conditional logic. The passage trains the student to handle conditional propositions with the same rigor as categorical syllogisms.

Key Terms
قد يكون (qad yakūn): It may be that; indicates a possibility in conditional propositions, equivalent to a particular affirmative in categorical logic. [Note: In some earlier texts, 'qad yakūn' is used for temporal possibility; here it is purely logical possibility.]
لا شيء (lā shay’): None; universal negative quantifier in the subject position of a conditional consequent. [Note: Same as in categorical logic; no significant shift.]
كل (kull): Every/all; universal affirmative quantifier. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
بعض (ba‘ḍ): Some; particular affirmative quantifier. [Note: Stable.]
سوالب (sawālib): Negative conditional propositions; the negation of the whole conditional, not just the consequent. [Note: In later works, 'sāliba' may distinguish between negation of the whole and negation of the consequent.]
خلف (khalf): Reductio ad absurdum; a proof by contradiction showing an assumption leads to a falsehood. [Note: Used widely in logic and jurisprudence; no significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a valid conditional possibility according to the passage?
A) If no A is B, then every C is D
B) If no A is B, then not every C is D
C) If every A is B, then no C is D
D) If some A is B, then every C is D

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions have exactly six valid quantifier patterns mapping antecedent to consequent, and implications among them can be proven by reductio, mirroring categorical logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Take the conditional statement: 'If some wine is intoxicating, then not every drink is permissible.' Identify which of the six affirmative possibilities it matches, and then write its negative counterpart as per the passage's pattern.
?
Task 2: Perform a reductio proof similar to the passage on the following: Assume 'Not every time that every A is B, then some C is D' implies 'It may be that if every A is B, then not some C is D'? Check whether the implication holds by attempting the proof.
